Why is Full Myth so awesome? I don't get it

\n
\n
\n
\n\n2097 \n\n\n
\n
\n\nJuly 2015 \n in Play Advice
\n
\n
\n
\n
\nWhen Jason recently posted that admonition, my heart sank because I felt so guilty.
But now I instead see opportunities to express ourselves in another way and more people can understand us.

When I play in a game that's realized in the Full Myth method -- that is to say with a predrawn, pre-populated map, regardless of whether my goal is to beat the dungeon, express my character, discover the secrets of the dungeon, or push my luck, I get the feeling that "Holy shit, this dungeon is real" in some sense, and I get kicks out of that.

How come I don't get the same kicks if the same dungeon is improvised, even if every word that's said at the game is the same?

I've told many times before the story that shocked my old No Myth -- that is to say, a fully improvised "from the top of my head" world -- ways out of me. We were playing D&D (Labyrinth Lord AEC to be precise) and we found a covered glass surface that we guessed was a mirror. We didn't look into the mirror. We strapped it on our "dungeonator" -- a siege wagon 10 foot wide that we had invented to use in the dungeon to fight skeletons -- and way later, extra-diegetical hours [that is to say, the clock on the wall in the playing room, not the hourglasses our player characters were using in the magical realm] had passed and we were on our way home to the City of Helix. We suddenly had to fight giant toad monsters!! We uncovered the mirror and one toad monster looked in it and burst like a bubble. The mirror cracked.

The exact same thing could've happened in a No Myth game where the DM made up things from their own imagination on the fly. They could've seen that we were scared of the mirror and after we revealed it to the toad monsters, "rewarded" us by giving it a harmful one-time effect on our foes. This was so cool so that when I got home I immediately called my own mom (for the record, we speak like six times a year or so so it's not like I'm a huge momma's girl) and told her the story.

As a middle ground, the mirror could've had an effect from a random table, rolled as we discovered it or as we uncovered it. This would still have been way way cooler than the DM making it up because the DM making it up makes us feel not free.

As it turns out, the DM has assured me that the mirror and it's effect were pre-placed on the dungeon map according to the module. To me this was amazing. The dungeon felt like a toy box. Like a real dungeon. The game as a whole -- rules, module, group, DM, dice -- together felt like a meaningful system, a system that stretched over both diegetic and extra-diegetic parts (that is to say, a "clock" where some "gears" were in the magical land and some "gears" were around the playing table).

Why is it so awesome that the mirror was placed in advance.
Why is it not awesome to make up such a mirror effect on the spot.

I have never railroaded (that is to say, have a pre-planned outcome or story written down and guided play to fit that pre-written story) but I have ran a lot, a lot, a lot of No Myth in my life. This experience with the mirror shocked my core and I became a believer in Full Myth at once.
Why is that?

Looking at external behaviours, we players are hearing and saying and doing the exact same things.

    \n I'll have to respond in more detail later, but the first thing that comes to mind is a counter-example of sorts: it was Tod (@AsIf) 's story about a Lady Blackbird game he ran which had a dragon trapped in a cage. The players figured out that they could use the dragon's breath (or something like that) to refuel the Owl and make their escape.

    They were thrilled at how perfectly the pieces fit together and that they had figured it out - a very high-level puzzle which must have been planned carefully from the start, with all the pieces in place.

    The way Tod tells the story, he didn't have the heart to tell them he'd improvised the whole thing and thrown in the dragon on a whim at the end.

    Moral of the story? Not so sure, but it seems relevant to this conversation.

    \n I dunno, Sandra! I think this is like me asking, "Why do people like beer? I can't stand it. I prefer wine." It's a matter of taste.

    I love games on all parts of the No-Myth to Full-Myth spectrum, though! So maybe I can help you understand what I like about Full Myth No Myth, anyway.

    To enjoy Full Myth No Myth, you have to care about things other than "this is real." That's all. When I play Fiasco, I don't care that we're making up everything as we go. I only care that the story we're creating a) makes a modicum of sense, and b) entertains us. I know that the Fiasco rules are going to deliver high drama, painful tragedy, dark comedy, and a few hours of solid fun. Fiasco doesn't even have a GM to "own" all the Myth setting and stuff, right? All issues of authority over the setting and characters are worked out at the table, without any rules, really. And it just works.

    Well, for me. You might not enjoy that kind of play. That's fine!

    \n As I remember the story, Paul, he did tell them and they got disappointed.\n
    When the game was over everybody kept talking about the dragon. I said "You know, that dragon was totally made up on the spot." They were flabbergasted. They thought it was in the game. In fact they thought saving it was part of the "plot" - and they thought that was cool. Except there is no plot, I reminded them. There's only like, 2 pages of rules. "But what about the steam being exactly enough to power the ship?" LB's Player asked. "That was just you doing a successful math roll and having a good idea," I answered. He smiled quizzically. "Huh," he said.

    At that moment I realized I had deflated his bubble.

    \n The "myth" discussed here originally means the "myth of the prepped world" or similar - wasn't it Seth Ben-Ezra who invented that term at the Forge, or am I misremembering? "No Myth" is supposed to mean a game where there is no "myth", the content is just being winged by whatever means. Sandra is using the terminology correctly, I think, and I at least understood immediately what she meant.

    Just like Adam, I am very fond of both ends of the myth spectrum and everything in between. A game like say Doctor Xaos (which we've been playing here recently) or Zombie Cinema is utterly minimalistic no myth design where everything there is to the game's scenario comes flying out of the frontal lobes of the players in whatever ugly or beautiful form it might take. Full Myth is great as well, I absolutely love having the firm and uncompromising truth of the scenario at my back when GMing e.g. D&D, as it provides me with a hygienic, immaculate immunity to doubt and self-censure as a referee - I'm not the one deciding on the lives and deaths, it's all in the Notes.

    Because you asked, Sandra, I'd hazard that the reason for why you like Full Myth better than No Myth could simply be that you've happened to encounter stronger games from one of the spectrum than the other. The way you describe having yourself ran a lot of No Myth feels familiar to me, as I started GMing that way as a youth, and sort of burned out on it, only regaining interest with more advanced Forgite techniques that still generally kept to the low end of the Myth spectrum, but simply made for more interesting games. I could imagine myself thinking that Full Myth wasn't for me, sort of opposite to your idea, if I hadn't delved into old school D&D over the last decade.

    Alternatively, maybe it's just the joy of discovering something new. I know that I was crazy happy for several years after I cracked the D&D code, couldn't get enough of the stuff. As we grow older, that feeling of fresh discovery and sense of wonder gets lamentably rare. So maybe Full Myth is so awesome because it's new (to you). This sentiment certainly reflects the joy some people have felt in the reverse situation, when they discover the effortless freedoms and opportunities of No Myth after being stifled by a tradition that emphasized the opposite.

    \n\nIsaac \n\n\n

    \n "Full Myth" makes sense if you take it in the sense of "There's a fully worked out mythology behind the places we visit" rather than the "The prior existence of the things we invent is fully a false myth". Which may be a bit misleading, though I can't at the moment think of a better term to describe a fully prepped gestalt.

    If I had to stab at a guess, the pleasure of Full Myth/Consistency-in-Detailed-Prep/Living-in-a-world comes from the sense of agency you get that your actions meant something: that you could have chosen differently.

    Having the consistency in the world means that your diegetic choices in-fiction mean something, whereas if the in-fiction diegetic choice was actually decided by the metafictional choice of your GM making an extra-diegetic choice to reward your characters' diegetic caution.

    I think it can work on different aspects of the game, too: For a game where you care deeply about the position of objects in space, working out a detailed floor plan for the dungeon is probably conducive to this, for a game about character relationships, a detailed relationship map will help.

    I do find that, as a player, I want to be sure that the rules of the space we're playing in are followed, depending on the game. Fiasco or Microscope are all about making stuff up. But in Burning Wheel I want you to hit me with those Resource tests for my lifestyle maintenance. And in Dungeon World I don't want to be able to give aid to my friend if I'm already diegetically preoccupied and too far away--I want the GM to enforce some limits.

    On the other side, as a GM...personally, I like AW Fronts, because they're at just the right balance for my tastes between I-planned-this-future-thing and I-need-to-be-flexible-for-the-players.

    Hypothesis: No-myth pushes the collaborative rewarding-of-decisions into the group space. That moment where someone comes up with an idea in Microscope that just fits and everyone realizes that it perfectly articulates the theme we didn't even realize we were working towards? That's the mirror image of the Full-myth moment when the preexisting components click into place.

    (I liked the way you defined the No-Myth/Full-Myth thing cleanly in the first post, by the way. Though I guess it wasn't clear for everyone!)

    \n To be precise about the Lady Blackbird story (nice of you guys to remember that), the majority of the group was surprised that I had improvised that stuff, but not in a bad way. It was one particular Player who was crestfallen. I might add that this is a person who is highly meticulous, a little obsessive, with a very strong "left brain" (mathematics, statistics), with very little experience at tabletop but lots of experience in MMORPGs. He wanted it to have been a "No Myth" experience.

    To be precise about my personal druthers: I like it all. For instance: My AD&D world is a gigantic library of maps, charts, histories, it's Full Myth. I do maybe 2-4 hours of prep for every hour of play (sometimes crazymuch more, for days on end, without a session in sight). I've posted before about how I even went about creating cultural and linguistic geneologies - I run AD&D with the Myth Pedal pushed to the floor. When I run a one-shot module for a group involving newbies I usually run in this mode as well.

    But when I run AW or DayTrippers, I ease up on the Myth Pedal about halfway.

    When I run Lady Blackbird or Everyone is John, I ease up on the pedal all the way.

    It's fun to drive different types of vehicles.

    \n Eero, both your theories are certainly possible with the data I have. I cannot deny that. I've ran No Myth in an unsatisfying way and Full Myth in a very satisfying way. Same goes for being in the player role.

    Isaac's theory is of course more appealing to me given that it speaks to an inherent quality of Full Myth that differentiates it from No Myth even though there are similarities in the external behaviors, and I've had the same thoughts myself: that the difference is partially related to player agency. (That said -- if it's just about Agency, AW would feel as satisfying since it has a relatively high degree of agency.)

    \n\nRicardo Tavares \n\n\n

    \nCan I just say that I like these kind of topics where we sometimes get to question the things we like (or don't like)? A lot of RPG discussion that I find online is either with people that I completely agree with (and don't have much to add) or completely disagree (and get ignored/misinterpreted when I try to say anything).

    I've played both a lot of Full and No Myth and I've always loved the possibility of improvising a story during play (as opposed to reading Fighting Fantasy books or playing videogames). However, throughout the years, I've learned the difference between improvising by myself and improvising with others. In the first approach, I'm not any sort of genius so I really have to prep in order to improvise (meaning, I prime myself with inspirational media, write down random ideas, make lists of things, etc.). In the second approach, I need to rely on others that might learn and play the game with me (instead of me just running the game to the point where I almost play with myself).

    I'm not particularly excited with extensive world building or with memorizing a pre-built scenario (although I've done both many times). I am excited about learning who your character is and finding cool interesting questions that bring him or her to life. I also find it fair that both of us have the power to make this session awesome or a very dull affair. In my opinion, since Full Myth separates the characters in play from the fully pre-defined setting, the players have less agency than with a No Myth game where the story is born out of who the characters are, want and do.

    However, I also know that a lot people are not nearly as excited as I am about improvising a story together. Maybe they prefer solving puzzles. Maybe they are used to having the story come to them. Maybe it's not fun to run the risk of setting up a conflict that you yourself also have to resolve.

    The thing is, improvising with others only works for real if you have a good game and people who want to learn and play it. If you show up with your fresh copy of Night Witches and everybody goes "idunno" to all the questions, there's not much of a game there. On the other hand, your standard Full Myth D&D can power through all the "idunno"s as there is a bunch of pre-made content to engage with.

    \n\nAirk \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nJuly 2015 \n

    \n
    \n
    \n The thing that always blows my mind about this discussion is that, really, there's no such thing as "full myth" - that's why it's called a "myth" in the first place. The whole thing is basically a lie. No matter how thoroughly you prep it, it's NOT A WORLD. Maybe you can put in enough prep to fake it (Hence "Myth") but often you can't, because good god, players. :P Sooner or later you're going to need to extrapolate from what you've written down, which is EXACTLY THE SAME as extrapolating from stuff you just made up five minutes ago. Or you're going to have to reveal that it's all actually just painted set dressing and they think they're attacking the Real Rock Ridge but they're actually attacking the Fake Rock Ridge.

    You can do your best to play "full myth" but it's still a myth. It's still not real. It's still not complete. Writing stuff down doesn't make it more real. And, as has been pointed out by your very own example, 2097, a well run "No Myth" game is functionally indistinguishable from a well run "Myth" game. The only difference is in the minds of the players and when the GM has to put in their work.

    As a result, I think this discussion should be, frankly, irrelevant to players. They should assume whatever makes them happy. GMs can argue this if they want, but once again, the choice comes down to "whatever helps you sleep at night" and "Whatever is easier for you to run" moreso than any difference in the actual game experience.

    The whole thing is a psychological trick. It's like telling someone an object is "one of a kind" even when it's not to make them like it more. You'd better hope they never check out antiques roadshow or something.

    The best games are where the players and GM agree on what kind of game is being run, just so no one gets their feelings hurt, but that's the only benefit I see. I think it probably says something that "No myth" players are almost universally willing to wink and nod and say "Oh, of COURSE it's all real, Oh Great Game Master." but "Myth" players seem to feel wounded and betrayed if they see behind the curtain. I'm not sure what it says, but I'm sure it tells us something.
    \n

    \n Well, surely the GM's feelings matter, too? It feels entirely different to run with prep or without prep. These are GMing techniques that suit different circumstances and goals.

    I myself do not underwrite the idea that you can do whatever you want to me as a player as long as I don't know about it. For example, in old school D&D I absolutely must trust that the GM is not cheating behind the screen. Even uncertainty about it causes my play motivation to deteriorate, I withdraw from committing seriously to the challenges and start half-assing it. "Oh I guess I'll go wrestle with that giant, let's see if the GM has the balls to kill me." This is admittedly something of a paradoxical conundrum for refereed challenge-based more-or-less Full Myth play, as the GM cannot prove their fidelity by revealing their scenario without ruining the fog of war, but we make do with GM affirmations (as in, the GM periodically talks about their continuing commitment to fair and objective refereeing), occasional overview (e.g. a player not involved in an important situation checks over the GM's dice rolls and notes), post-action procedural checks (i.e. the GM explains what it was all about later) and just generally trusting each other to not cheat because we're friends and what would be the point.

    As a general issue of social philosophy I find that treating other people as objects you play psychological tricks upon runs afoul of Immanuel Kant's golden rule, which is a big deal for me, as I was a huge fan of Kant through my teenage years

    \n I can't respond to exactly what the OP is asking, but I can point out something in my own experience that may be somehow relevant.

    My tables are usually Full Myth. In fact my biggest enjoyment from most game systems is reading the setting flavor and detail. What's more, my games border upon what some consider railroading (though I don't personally take the definition to that much of an extreme). After much experimentation we've found that it works the best for us and except for occasional impromptu games, is what people come to the table expecting and wanting.

    This doesn't mean there's not improvisation, player agency, or broad options. It just means that my players have come to expect a certain style and level of preparatory commitment on my part with regard to plot, setting, dramatis personae, props, background, and so on.

    The reason for this is that my players tend to appreciate detail, subtlety, verisimilitude, and depth in games. Those are things that REQUIRE preparation. Even if you're very very smart and knowledgeable, creative as a profession and a talent, and have the best mechanical framework available you'll simply never achieve the breadth and depth of complexity from improvisation that you can with careful planning and sculpting. It's why you'll never see a big budget movie without some kind of working script and the ground work done on location scouting, casting, props, wardrobe, character development, etc. Whose Line is it Anyway is a hell of a fun show, but it could never compete in an award race with Lord of the Rings or Ben-Hur, nor achieve the lasting cultural impact of the Star Wars franchise (regardless of personal preferences).

    How many times during an improvised session have you thought 'oh man, this is an awesome development...if only I'd thought of it back in scene X I could have laid this foundation for it and everyone would be completely amazed right now and we'd have this whole other thing to explore'?

    Fully fleshed out NPC with traits and realism, deep sub-plots, inter-connectivity, detailed puzzles or riddles or functional details even on flavor items, foreshadowing, perfection of language or mood, etc. These are the hallmarks of great Full Myth that are, at best, random and accidental with No Myth.

    Now, your mileage may well vary, but even though my tables have contained professional writers, actors, artists, lifelong gamers and developers, and exceptional super-geniuses, with most of the games ever written available to us, this has held true for us over 35 years.

    The only real caveat to this is warrant. If you're not interested in those aspects of gaming to begin with, then obviously it wouldn't be beneficial to you no matter how true it is in general.

    But that's why it's usually going to be Full Myth for us.

    \n
    Well, surely the GM's feelings matter, too? It feels entirely different to run with prep or without prep. These are GMing techniques that suit different circumstances and goals.
    \nRight, which is why I said the GM should choose this based on whatever helps them sleep at night and whatever they find easier to run. If a GM wants to prep a ton of stuff, either because they believe that writing it down somehow makes it "official" or whether they just find it hard to make stuff up on the fly, good for them. But I don't think that really matters from a myth/no myth standpoint.

    Also, I sortof disagree with phoenix182 that creating detailed and well fleshed out NPCs is even an example of "Full Myth" play. I don't think it is. Full Myth implies the creation of the entire world, or at least, the entire "place" that is being explored. Having an idea of the personalities and motivations of your NPCs is something that is actually probably even MORE useful if you're going to be called on to make up a lot of stuff.

    \n\n2097 \n\n\n

    \n\nJuly 2015 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n Full Myth and No Myth are both unattainable ideals. When attempting Full Myth, there are gonna be holes. When attempting No Myth, there is gonna be preconcieved notions.

    I'm interested in techniques to patch full myth's holes. Random tables and \u201dluck checks" are among the most obvious ones.

    For me also, some areas of the game are more important to be full myth than others. Like in the mirror story, the color of the cloth I wouldn't have minded if it was unspecified in the text. If the color is immediately relevant, you can roll for a random color. If not, make it up. The mirror's magical effect on the other hand I see as more salient, that is to say I care more about it.

    \n I agree that the "why is random less bad than DM deciding" is a productive line of reasoning. It's interesting in both directions: "why is prepared better than random" and/or "why is random better than DM deciding."

    Let's say the players find a bookshelf. The players grab one book.

    \u2022 You have the whole shelf mapped out so you ask "OK which shelf, and counting from the left edge, which book?"
    \u2022 You have a list of ten random titles specific to this room
    \u2022 You have a list of ten random titles that is supposed to cover all bookshelfs in the entire world
    \u2022 You have one title in mind and tell that to the players.

    I think I've sorted them from "unreasonable" to "too much control". The random method falls in the middle.

    Why is the latter case, having one title in mind, "too much control"?
    It's because you can decide on a title that suits the moment -- and that's what chafes for me. It's too clever. It's too dramatic. It's too \u0427\u0435\u0301\u0445\u043e\u0432's Gun.

    The mirror is another example. Maybe if we had immediately looked in the mirror, the DM would've decided that we could scry another world. But since we were scared of the mirror, the DM got influenced to think of it as a weapon, to have the toad monster vanish like a bubble. The DM riffing on our decision, playing along with us like jazz -- sounds cool, but makes our decisions meaningless. We found a magic mirror and we decided what to do with it as it were, without knowing what it was.
    And the module writer put it in there without knowing what we would do with it (but I guess he had some ideas). And our DM got to watch it all.

    I got the feeling of really finding a magic mirror and to see what I (or rather, my level 1 monk, A'ina) would've done in that situation.

    \n I have a sorta OCD for pleasing people. I feel both guilty about going full myth or no myth. When I go full myth I end up railroading (I've always been sublte and logic enough that my players have never complain about it anyway) because I often don't have enough time to prep a whole detailed world. When I go no myth, it's as phoenix182 says and my scenes lack substance, depth, etc.

    But that's when I go No Myth alone.

    I agree that it requires the GM to get used to it; it's part Play Unsafe, part brainstorming and part prep. It's a lot easier than going full myth but again, getting used to it is the hard part. What I do is create the whole setting with the players and hear their ideas about what they want to find in this world. Then in prep I flesh an connect those ideas into encounters, stats, characters, places and monsters.

    This way players get to see what they want and their foreknowledge of it acts as their "character knowledge" about the matter. I often don't have to narrate what the PCs know about something after they roll their (proper knowledge skill) but instead the rest of the players get to hear the creator of that bit go "GASP!" and then quickly explain what they "know" about it. In play it works like if both the player and the GM did prep without being exhaustive about it.

    It doesn't save them from surprises; I clearly state when they give me their input that what they are telling me is what their character knows and actual truth in the setting may differ. Also, I don't often let them know exactly when I'll be using their input. For best effect I use it when they are distracted with something or expecting something else.

    I also tend to turn criticals and fumbles into story. Like, if one unimportant NPC or enemy unlikely evades several attacks, I narrate how they see something different about this one, and maybe try to make it escape the current battle so the party finds it later in a more important position and with some additional background or at least description. A bugbear that survived two encounters with the party and almost TPKed the players then now is the only clean, elegant and cultured bugbear in the setting (they saw him coming out of his bath and politely speaking with another npc before the third encounter)

    I feel like at last I've found my own playing style, my own way to compromise between full Myth and No Myth.

    \n This is why I mentioned the incident with Lady Blackbird and the dragon at the start of this thread:

    It's really key, I feel, to understand (or at least explore) which aspects of this whole "Myth" thing scratch your creative itch. Some examples, each with a Devil's Advocate hypothetical (if I can think of one):

    * Does the fun come from the feeling you have as a GM, or interfacing with something real and concrete, and presenting it to the players?

    (In this case, would you enjoy it just as much if you had a phone line to another GM, who told you precisely what was in each room, and you just relayed it to the players? What if you knew that this other GM was making it all up as she went?)

    * Does the fun come from knowing something about the *quality* of the material itself? Knowing that it's self-consistent, or balanced in regard to game challenge, or some other similar quality, like artistic genius beyond your own creative abilities?

    (Would you ever insist that your GM run a game from a module, or base the game in a fictional world, because you want a higher quality of game material than what you feel the GM can produce? Would you accept such a statement from your players?)

    * As a player, does the fun come from immersing in the world as though it was real, and *knowing* that it's not being made up on the spot, which gives your victories a certain legitimacy?

    (In this case, how would you feel if you were playing in four different D&D games, but before the games started, the GMs drew straws, and ONE of them had to improvise everything, while the other two played from notes? If you didn't know which one was real and which one was not, would it influence your enjoyment of the game? Or is it being likely enough?)

    * As a player, does the fun come from a lack of trust for your GM? (i.e. You don't trust them enough to improvise material that's fair, or good, or high-quality, or some other feature.)

    (Is there a way you can build this trust over time? Speaking for myself, I can say that Eero's various devices - described by him a few posts back - really work in this regard.)

    And so on...

    \n Another interesting question is the myth of "how long ago" the material was 'mythed up'.

    Let's say you think it's really cool to deal with a mirror puzzle, because it was written in a module 20 years ago. It seems "real" in this sense; other players have dealt with it too, in the same form!

    But you don't think it's cool to deal with a mirror which the GM JUST NOW decided was a teleportation mirror, because she did so with full knowledge of your prior actions and some of your possible biases.

    Ok, what if the GM came up with the mirror concept last week?

    At the end of the last session, at home?

    During the break in your game session tonight, just 20 minutes ago?

    While you were discussing how to break into the room which holds the mirror?

    The moment you looked upon the mirror?

    The GM hasn't decided yet, but will in a moment, based on a random table.

    The GM hasn't decided yet, but will in a moment, based on some other form of judgement.

    Why is the latter case, having one title in mind, "too much control"?

    Just "a title that suits the moment" indeed has no depth, but that's on the GM to bring something to the table (like everybody else does) and on the game to incorporate all these possible inputs into something that's fun to play.

    \n Running a module makes the DM even more of a "neutral third party" and the module writer becomes "the adversary". So that's a very big plus for that. But it's not usually higher quality or more interesting or flavorful.\n
    In this case, would you enjoy it just as much if you had a phone line to another GM, who told you precisely what was in each room, and you just relayed it to the players? What if you knew that this other GM was making it all up as she went?
    \nOK, that is a pretty shocking and interesting thought experiment.
    Scenario 1:
    The co-GM on the other side of the line cannot see the players?
    The co-GM doesn't know what the players are doing?
    It's like:
    Local DM: "OK, co-GM... what are some of the books on the shelf in that room?"
    co-GM: "Hmm... one is the Joy of Spidersilk Gardening, the next one is Volo's Guide to the North".


    Later:
    Local DM: "OK, co-GM... what happens if someone looks in the mirror?"
    co-GM: "Let's say they disappear heh heh! What, did a player look?
    Local DM: "We can talk about that later. Now just focus on making things up. I am the referee, you are the channeler".

    I.e. the set up is that the co-GM doesn't know what is happening and what the players are doing. And the co-GM writes everything down so it can fit into a logical and coherent world view.

    Scenario 2:

    Local DM: "OK, a player just lifted up the cloth and peeked in the mirror. What happens?"
    co-GM: "Oh, it's not dangerous, but they can scry an eldritch world!"

    This scenario 2 is not ok. As for scenario 1... I might have to sleep on it. Very interesting.

    To me, my reaction to this experiment suggests that separation between "prep" and "running" is a vital part of the appeal of Full Myth.\n
    As a player, does the fun come from immersing in the world as though it was real, and *knowing* that it's not being made up on the spot, which gives your victories a certain legitimacy?
    \nI believe so.\n
    In this case, how would you feel if you were playing in four different D&D games, but before the games started, the GMs drew straws, and ONE of them had to improvise everything, while the other two played from notes? If you didn't know which one was real and which one was not, would it influence your enjoyment of the game? Or is it being likely enough?

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n

    \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
    \n

    \n
    \n As an aside, I can't let this go by without some comment (hopefully not a total derail):\n

    The reason for this is that my players tend to appreciate detail, subtlety, verisimilitude, and depth in games. Those are things that REQUIRE preparation. Even if you're very very smart and knowledgeable, creative as a profession and a talent, and have the best mechanical framework available you'll simply never achieve the breadth and depth of complexity from improvisation that you can with careful planning and sculpting. It's why you'll never see a big budget movie without some kind of working script and the ground work done on location scouting, casting, props, wardrobe, character development, etc. Whose Line is it Anyway is a hell of a fun show, but it could never compete in an award race with Lord of the Rings or Ben-Hur, nor achieve the lasting cultural impact of the Star Wars franchise (regardless of personal preferences).
    \nThe assumption that preparation leads to better results is not necessarily one which should go unexamined.

    Why do I say that? I kind of have to. I'm a professional jazz musician, and a sometimes composer. So how do I decide whether I want to write a piece of music note-for-note, or give space to the musicians to improvise? Here, in this spot? How about here?

    There are lots of great composers who have learned that, with the right musicians, your piece will sound better if you allow creative space instead of writing exactly what should happen.

    It's certainly not as simple as:

    * Preparation means high-quality content.
    * Improvisation saves you time and effort, but at the cost of lower-quality content.

    Not at all. If that were the case, jazz as a genre would not exist.

    And why do a lot of bands sound better live than on their recordings, even though recordings typically (especially in the modern era) "fix" all the mistakes, and are arguably just better products, more carefully considered?

    Consider for a minute that you wanted to have an important conversation with someone. Perhaps it's a date. Or a job interview. Or you're coming out of the closet and you need to tell your family.

    Do you prepare beforehand, writing out *exactly* what you're going to say? Or would you rather speak as it is seems right in the moment?

    (Also, are those the only two options? Or are there lots of hubrid forms, like a checklist of things you need to say, which you can go through in any order you like?)

    I think I speak for most of us when I say that we wouldn't want to bring a script on a first date with someone we like. Why is that? Surely, if you prepared beforehand, you could make a better impression?

    Well, turns out that improvising carries a lot of advantages which are lost when material is prepared beforehand. Adapting and adjusting to the other party. Being able to take advantage of inspiration. Being able to build on something the other party brings to the table. A general sense of honesty and connection. Responsiveness. Intimacy. (That's why you don't bring a script on your date, after all.)

    We also just plain *listen* better when we're not sticking to a script. When you're following a pre-set formula, your brain is multi-tasking and you don't pay as much attention to the people you are working with - it blinds you to a certain extent.

    All of these things are lost when you stick to prepared material - it's a trade-off, not just a question of convenience and saving time.

    I was really shocked when I read "Well of Souls", one of the first public "Narrativist" adventure scenarios. Why was I shocked? First of all, it didn't plan out any future events; just NPC motivations, background details, and so forth. Second, and worse, it had a big revelation built into the plot (I don't remember if it was a murder or a wedding, but there was something which happened, and it could have been one of two different people). And it didn't specify which one it was!

    What?!? What's the point of leaving that out of the scenario?

    I later realized that this was very, very intentional. The idea was to see how the game developed, what themes were present, and then be able to choose, in the moment, the revelation which would be the most hard-hitting, or the one most likely to create the best play going forward. If your goal is surprise, you can achieve it almost every time. If your goal is to complicate the situation, you can do so.

    Imagine playing Star Wars. You've decided that Darth Vader is secretly Anakin's son. Ooh, this is going to be a cool revelation! And you can foreshadow the crap out of it.

    But what happens if Anakin's player is just not interested in Darth Vader? He's obsessed with his relationship to Obi-Wan, instead, and that starts taking over the game.

    What if you started the game with the knowledge that Anakin is a foster kid, and we don't know much about who his dad was beyond some rumours. The player is obviously into exploring this (maybe even came up with it himself). We start to build some foreshadowing around that: here are some hints about how Darth Vader could be his dad... but here are also some hints that Obi-Wan treats him a little too much like he was family, and now we find an old book in the attic of the farm which appears to be written by a relative of Anakin, but it's spelled with the family name Palpatine.

    In play, we can pick the moment to reveal that the Emperor, or Obi-Wan, or Vader... is the father! Or maybe that it was all misguided and his "uncle" is actually his real father, after all. Or that he was cloned, and doesn't have a family, but his genes were constructed out of bio-samples from all the greatest Jedi in the galaxy by his creepy uncle.

    That's a very different kind of potential. It could fall flat, but it also could allow us to reach greater heights of drama in a different way. And the player can be involved in it, too, without necessarily spoiling the surprise.

    So, what's the point?

    * Improvisation is not necessarily lower-quality than preparation. We have entire artistic fields (like jazz, some forms of rap, some forms of acting, etc) which demonstrate that spontaneous creation can be TRAINED. You can learn to improvise at just as high a level as people write/prepare/etc.

    I'm playing a show tonight with a trumpet player who is a competent but not brilliant improviser. When we recorded an album this winter, he wrote complex and beautiful solos to play and learned them. They are arguably much better than what he normally plays. But, playing "from the page", his playing lots its spark. His time feel wasn't as good, there was less emotional impact in his playing, and he didn't execute the lines with his usual intensity. I could instantly hear that he wasn't improvising, and it just didn't have the usual impact.

    And, significantly, whole groups can learn to do this. Music is no simpler than gaming (or, at least, it's on a similar plane, if you disagree with that), but I've seen musicians and groups of musicians learn to improvise with tremendous complexity: there is all the same sense of structure and depth, but with the added spark of spontaneous creation.

    I've also seen groups of gamers improvise fiction on a level far better than most modules or scenarios or plots I've seen. "No Myth" allows you to explore that spectrum

    \n This isn't unique to music or roleplaying. Some actors value spontaneous techniques like "method acting". Some of our favourite lines in cinema were improvised. And novel writers are always debating whether it's better to write from a plot you've designed from the start (e.g. Jim Butcher) or if fiction is more powerful when it's allowed to grow organically (e.g. Stephen King), and I'd venture to say that most of the "greats" belong to the second school of thought (although I don't have proof).

    After all, otherwise we could argue that if the *players* knew everything ahead of time - including what their characters were going to do, when they would die, and so forth - they could play their characters better, and the games would be more "high-quality". They could foreshadow, include more details, you know, *really* get into it, and drive the game to new heights...

    (Sorry for the lengthy aside. But it seems relevant, at least somewhat.)

    \nI just think it feels way way cooler to find out what the pre-written relationship is than to make a relationship up that "fits" play.

    Have you guys played the board game Zendo? You have a riddle and you try to find out what that riddle is. Pretty fun, easy game.
    Also Netrunner or Ghosts. You hide a trap in one server (or a red ghost in one corner) and an agenda in another server (or a blue ghost in the other corner) and you try to fake the other player out. Love it. Feels satisfying to find out.
    I get the kicks.
    But... if it's just "oh I'll put the trap in whatever server you run hahaha" it becomes meaningless. I don't get the kicks.

    This isn't unique to music or roleplaying. Some actors value spontaneous techniques like "method acting". Some of our favourite lines in cinema were improvised. And novel writers are always debating whether it's better to write from a plot you've designed from the start (e.g. Jim Butcher) or if fiction is more powerful when it's allowed to grow organically (e.g. Stephen King), and I'd venture to say that most of the "greats" belong to the second school of thought (although I don't have proof).

    (Sorry for the lengthy aside. But it seems relevant, at least somewhat.)
    \nAs someone who wrote her novel by following the instructions in On Writing to the letter, I know about King. (The novel turned out really bad though!)

    But I feel like we're in a couple of misconceptions here.
    1. It's not about prepared plot. It's about prepared place. Items, people. I didn't do this when writing my book (I wrote it No Myth then, like the King says, waited two weeks then went back and deleted 10%). But I enjoy it when playing RPGs.

    2. RPGs aren't always about story. I love the website story-games.com/forums but I am not necessarily into "story" in gaming. I think Adam and Sage made a good choice when they deliberately avoided that word in making Dungeon World.

    \n
    Also Netrunner or Ghosts. You hide a trap in one server (or a red ghost in one corner) and an agenda in another server (or a blue ghost in the other corner) and you try to fake the other player out. Love it. Feels satisfying to find out.
    I get the kicks.
    But... if it's just "oh I'll put the trap in whatever server you run hahaha" it becomes meaningless. I don't get the kicks.
    \nI'm getting the sense that, at least for you, this is the fulcrum of the Full Myth/No Myth issue: something about having the answer be a quantum state that only collapses when observed rubs you the wrong way.

    \n\nAirk \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nJuly 2015 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n I don't agree that "No Myth" is unattainable at all - that's kindof the point. All you need for "No myth" play is the admission that, hey, this stuff isn't real, I haven't made it all up ahead of time, so it's going to be stuff I make up as I go along. That doesn't mean the GM is going to be inventing random stuff or just spouting gibberish, that everything is going to be completely disconnected. It just means the GM hasn't plotted out the layout of the Tower of Evil, or established the distance from Lake Town to Woodsville. "Full Myth" is, in fact, a myth because you can't make up everything but - and this is the important but - you are PRETENDING that you can/have. There is no such pretending in No Myth. There's just no reason for it to be there.

    I think, humorously, that randomness is also basically No Myth - as soon as you admit that you're rolling to find out what's there, you might as well just be making it up. There's no longer anything "real" by any stretch, and the dice are just as arbitrary as a person. I guess there's some weird conception that the dice are more "fair" than a person, but they're also more DUMB. And anyway, who is populating the "table" the dice are picking from? The GM. So if he really wants something to happen, that table can easily look like "Okay, 1-19, it's ogres, on a 20, it's goblins." or whatever.

    No, you don't "put the trap wherever the PCs go"; Because by the time the PCs decide to go there, you should have already decided where the trap is. I think there's this huge misconception about "No Myth" that gets trotted out every time someone wants to criticize it. People act like everything is invented right there as it happens. And this is completely false. Say the PCs come to a four way intersection, so they have three choices (having already approached from one side). They can go North, West, or South. The GM doesn't wait for the players to decide "Okay, we go South" and then scramble to think of what's down the South corridor. The GM instead thinks "Okay, they're at an intersection. South is a dead end with a pit trap, North leads to the dungeon, where there are probably some guards and West is towards the Warlock's Sanctum, so there's going to be some magical wards." So there's no "Oh, you picked South? Okay, there's a pit trap!" - the pit trap was already there before the PCs went that way, but it was there 20 seconds before, and not 20 hours before. The fact that GM wrote it down the night more doesn't make it any more or less true than the version the GM decided 20 seconds before. Similarly, with your example with the mirror - in a "No Myth" situation, the GM isn't going to wait for a player to look into the mirror before he decides what it does. He's going to decide that beforehand, so that he knows how to deal with it if the PCs do, or don't, or throw a goblin in front of it, or whatever.

    I think that misconception that everything is being made up "on the spot" means that everything is being made up the very moment it is needed is fundamental to your problems with "No Myth". And while I guess I can't promise you that no one runs a game like that, it seems absurd that someone would try.
    \n

    \n Isaac: you're right, but I want to phrase it as a positive. It's less about collapsing the waveform rubbing me the wrong way and more about the tangible, \u201dreal" experience feeling good.

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nJuly 2015 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n

    But I feel like we're in a couple of misconceptions here.
    1. It's not about prepared plot. It's about prepared place. Items, people. I didn't do this when writing my book (I wrote it No Myth then, like the King says, waited two weeks then went back and deleted 10%). But I enjoy it when playing RPGs.

    2. RPGs aren't always about story. I love the website story-games.com/forums but I am not necessarily into "story" in gaming. I think Adam and Sage made a good choice when they deliberately avoided that word in making Dungeon World.
    \nHeh! I was sure you were going to say that.

    First of all, some of the stuff I'm talking about DOES suit narrativist/intense-stories kind of stuff particularly well.

    If I were playing a competitive game where I try to outsmart my opponent I certainly wouldn't see the point if they could move around their pieces after-the-fact.

    All very true, and very real tendencies.

    However, it doesn't negate my point. Here's a D&D example:

    * We're playing a bunch of badass adventurers, and exploring a "sandbox"-style world. The concept is heavy on the undead paradigm: zombies, liches, all that kind of stuff.

    * One day, one of our characters levels up and gets a really powerful ability or magical item, which allows us to control undead.

    * Suddenly, all the undead threats are not challenging anymore.

    What do we do now?

    There's a total possibility in a "No Myth", fully-scripted world that something like this happens:

    1) Since the concept of the campaign was otherworldly demonic forces from the underworld breaking into 'reality', there are undead everywhere. We've short-circuited the entire campaign; nothing is a challenge anymore.

    2) We've got this awesome ability or artifact, but it turns out there are no undead anywhere on the continent where we live. (Except we don't know that, or only the GM knows.)

    Neither is terribly satisfying, most likely. (For the sake of this horribly exaggerated example; clearly it's hyperbole. Please don't poke holes in the example - I'm just trying to illustrate how non "story-oriented" concerns can come out of flexibility/prep.)

    Embracing aspects of "No Myth" play allows you, as a group, to say, "OK, so what WOULD be an interesting challenge for this group?" Maybe it's traveling elsewhere, maybe it's finding sources of challenge where there are no undead. Clearly the undead thing is basically solved; so what's next?

    Or maybe the entire focus of the campaign shifts. This otherworldly demonic threat can now be handled; the PCs become some of the most important people in the campaign, and are asked to rule. Now we need to come up with relationship maps or new otherworldly forces, or perhaps some details on how the government in this world is run... something else.

    'No Myth' allows to go there, effortlessly. The hypothetical 'Full Myth' (which, of course, doesn't exist in reality) COULD potentially leave us stranded with a whole book of information that's no longer relevant to our campaign, or no longer a challenge to our characters, or simply no longer in line with our interests.

    Now we have to improvise.
    \n

    \n Airk. We're obviously defining the phrase \u201dNo Myth" differently, you and I. Let's get on the same page with terminology before continuing.
    If I am talking about a DM deciding on the mirror's effect just when it's gazed into, that's what I want to contrast with the DM reading the mirror's effect in the module and obeying that. Then one of the two can be called No Myth, Full Myth, Ducks, Doves, or Giraffes for all I care.
    And sure, let's "include the excluded middle" for when it's relevant. The DM deciding on the mirror's effect as it's found, for example. That's interesting to discuss also.
    But don't semantics trick it so that one case becomes a stand-in for the other. I wanted to discuss the specific cases of pre-written mirror vs post-hoc mirror. I find the crux there.

    \n Bad example. It's not "No Myth" to shift a campaign's focus by having the characters travel to continue their crusade against undead, or by having the characters turn to other concerns once the undead threat is no more. It is, in fact, an entirely ordinary turn of events in a sandbox game for a party to find a powerful magic item that they do not currently have an use for, and then throw it away or sell it or do whatever they want with it; the game goes on regardless, an organic dead end like that is just something that happens all the time if you accept the sandboxing creative premise (that is, that you're willing to have dead ends and narratives that never finish for the sake of added authenticity and meaningfulness to those strands that do finish up).

    Why is Full Myth so awesome? I don't get it

    \n
    \xab123456\xbb

      \n
      \n Definitionally, there are games on the full spectrum from zero preparation to full preparation.

      No prep - Games like Fiasco and A Thousand and One Nights where anything can be invented; or more traditional game-mastered games where the GM is making things up on the spot. Beyond tabletop RPGs, this connects to acting improv games and storytelling games like Once Upon a Time.

      Full prep - A GM uses a mega-dungeon module, and the players run through that for several months, then they end the campaign when they finish. Beyond tabletop RPGs, this connects to larps and online RPGs, where often no background or locations are created on the spot.
      \n

      \n I'm with Phoenix 182 because even with the jazz metaphor, there's some sort of prep done. The session is an improvisation, all right, but all players are aware of it and come with some idea for inspiration; instruments are prepared and according to what you brought, everyone will adapt and play something different. But well, basically my point is that learning to improvise is just prepping in a different way.

      I'm speaking from experience. When I got burnt out as a GM a few years ago, I was exhausted of doing prep and started researching many different ways of improvising things for games. From generators and random tables of any kind to returning the questions to the players and othe tricks. It took a while but I finally realized that even after some training, my best games had been by far the ones where I did some responsible prep, like learning the best tricks to improvise stuff, make my own random tables or devices and taking in consideration the players interests and draw inspiration from there.

      But well I consider prep even getting to know most of the rules by memory instead of just learning how to play and GM the game, because the former speeds up gameplay greatly and being self-confident in front of the players about this particular makes everybody feel a bit more relaxed at the table about just playing and letting the GM take care of the mechanical stuff.

      Definitions of Prep aside; as a GM this is why I feel ok with Illusionism. Either if it was in the prep twenty years ago or if it was made up just now, what matters more to me is the effect it produces on the players. Their Suspension of Disbelief allows us to have fun, so as a GM I prefer to respect that and don't just tell them if it was made up while I was at the toilet. Unless they ask (which is often because part of it felt improvised, which would be my fault.)

      \n
      As an aside, I can't let this go by without some comment (hopefully not a total derail):

      The assumption that preparation leads to better results is not necessarily one which should go unexamined.

      \nYou're absolutely right, and I phrased that whole aspect very badly.

      I didn't mean to imply that one gives an objectively better result, nor that one can't produce positive results at all.

      What I was trying to say is that within a given subjective preference of outcomes one method will achieve greater degrees of success more consistently...at least in my experience. For people that like the things I talked about my tables enjoying they're going to be achieved more often with careful, detailed prep than with improvisation (for the reasons I touched on before).

      To apply this to your music example, not everyone likes jazz (or even live tracks). There's a reason the overwhelming percentage of musical pieces on radio (or modern equivalents) are studio tracks. Music appreciation is subjective, much like rpgs. Any particular deviation may be more pleasing to some, but is just as likely to be less pleasing to an equivalent set.

      As for your 'script or no-script' example, I would script it. Absolutely, every time. I may deviate from the script once begun, but I would NEVER attempt to say anything important unless I'd scripted it fully first. It's going to achieve better results more often (providing the point was to say specific things in specific ways) when scripted, which is why you're taught to do this in communication/speech classes. On a first date? You're not trying to say a specific thing in a specific way. You don't yet know your audience, or even necessarily the topics. It's an interview, not a dramatic piece. A movie or book is generally different. Now some people may prefer their RPGs to be interviews, but if they prefer them to be movies they'll like it better more often with a script.

      Again, I'm sure there may be exceptions, but in 35+ years with groups including professional authors, actors, creators, intellectuals, etc, I have NEVER seen an improvisation as broad and deep as a prepared piece. Not one time. I've seen very good improv, and very fun improv. I've seen a deep moment, or one broad aspect. But never the kind of consistent vast detail and intricacy that is achieved through careful preparation. Again, I'm not talking objectively better, but subjectively better at achieving specific goals.

      I have a feeling it's largely a general personal tendency too...for instance, while I can enjoy jazz I far prefer rote classical. While I can enjoy improv I far prefer a scripted movie. While I can enjoy spontaneous poetry I far prefer a known sonnet. The things that I enjoy are exact things that require preparation...practiced precision. It's who I am in life, so it's who I am at the gaming table.

      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n No one should ever try to GM Full Myth if they don't enjoy it. For GMs who do enjoy it, though, there are certain specific benefits for players. Just because improv GMing is also awesome doesn't mean it covers the same bases as Full Myth play.

      Here's an example: I've been in the position of trying to fake Full Myth play for players who were used to it with other GMs. I liked Full Myth but I didn't enjoy every bit of the work my predecessors had done, so I did my best to cheat. In the process, I discovered what did and didn't work for my group of Full Myth-loving players.

      First, one note:
      When I've described this in the past, some people have said, "Those players are needlessly picky and just want what they're used to for no other reason than familiarity." I disagree. Picky? Yes. Needlessly? No. Their requirements do impact the roleplay experience in tangible ways.

      So, here's what worked for me, as GM:
      - Looking up fictional facts is fine.
      - Rolling to evaluate in-fiction probabilities (does it rain today?) is fine as long as they're well-modeled (don't forget, we're in a low-rain terrain).
      - Stating with complete certainty what happens without any look-ups or rolls is fine.
      - Any one of these eventually ceases to be fine if it shows any pattern of producing implausible nonsense, but "simple statement" is on the shortest leash.
      - Pretending to look up fictional facts or pretending to roll for probabilities while actually making stuff up and then stating it with complete certainty is fine if I don't get caught making stuff up. Giveaways are things like far-away glances which betray creative thought.
      - Asking players for established fictional info is fine, as long as I can seamlessly fall back to an appropriate Plan B if they don't remember the info. If they don't remember whether the info was ever established, I can look-up or roll. If they remember the info was established but not what it was, I must look-up.
      - Anything else is not fine. (By which I mainly mean transparent invention.)

      Why (this type of) Full Myth is so awesome:
      The advantage to playing this way is hard to explain, but concerns the weight of the fictional world and the realness of the events within, both during play and after play. It's perhaps less important to in-the-moment fiction-based satisfaction than it is to a sense of real-world permanence and achievement over time. In imprecise shorthand: "There's a big difference between changing (through roleplaying in it) a world that comes and goes and changing a world that's solid and persistent."
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      (My point being that codifying everything in advance is always going to be a trade-off at some level, and that elements of "No Myth" give a game flexibility, responsiveness, and the ability to adapt.)

      (I don't think that it's a failure of the game if a sandbox D&D GM shifts around some of the locations of module adventure in the game world in order to create a game which better suits the interests of the group, for example, especially if those things hadn't been established already, and the GM isn't doing it in order to fulfill some other agenda. I saw you doing lots of this kind of stuff in our IRC D&D game - for example, offering adventure hooks in response to player interests and such.)
      \nAh, yes - that's why I'm so agnostic on this matter, in part. While I appreciate a lot what is possible near the ends of this continuum, in reality Full Myth runs into some drastic difficulties if one tries to take it as some sort of an absolute guideline. It's not a value in itself, one might say.

      Considering my D&D style, for instance, you know how I sermonize about the "logistics phase" and "negotiation of challenge"? That's basically the No Myth part of the game, at a strategic level, where we acknowledge openly that this is not a simulation, people, we're not just running a medieval sandbox simulation for the fun of it; we're adventure gaming, and this means that it is OK for the GM to provide demographically unlikely hooks (you don't need to roll to have a wounded messenger for the crown prince stumble into the tavern just as the PCs happen to be there), players to have their characters to stupid shit to stumble into adventure (players who voluntarily have their characters get drunk and wake up in chains on a slave ship do not need to fear an unsportsmanlike quick death after that - they'll get at least a sporting chance), and so on and so forth.

      Later on, as we drill down and recognize the challenge, the Myth sets, so to speak - freedoms that were necessary and desirable while still negotiating the challenge would be unacceptable arbitrary decision-making when the stakes are high and heat is on.

      There is no contradiction to having the same game feature high and low arbitrariness at different times, because No Myth vs. Full Myth is only a continuum in the abstract; in reality we have various stages of the game that are set up in different ways, and prepped for with different techniques, and we can thus only talk about the Myth level in the aggregate. As Sandra describes, of course she doesn't mean by "Full Myth" that she tries to simulate everything - nobody could do that and run an interesting game at the same time. It's just a matter of relative emphasis, whether you've got more Myth than some other game might.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Re: script vs improv, my position is that, absent some really coercive GMing, there will always be tons of improv; no amount of scripting will destroy that. For example, even the most script-reliant GM's NPC reaction to a witty player character one-liner will be impromptu, and the difference between a laugh and a stare-down can color that NPC forever after. So unless the player is prevented from throwing out a one-liner, we've got some jazz in there.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

    • \n
      \n

      \n
      \n\nphoenix182 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Re: script vs improv, my position is that, absent some really coercive GMing, there will always be tons of improv; no amount of scripting will destroy that. For example, even the most script-reliant GM's NPC reaction to a witty player character one-liner will be impromptu, and the difference between a laugh and a stare-down can color that NPC forever after. So unless the player is prevented from throwing out a one-liner, we've got some jazz in there.
      \nOh absolutely. Not to mention that no matter how prepared you are, the players are GOING to do it different than you ever imagined. When I talk about preparation it's more about static setting elements, plot lines, contingencies, mannerisms of npcs and qualities of props, mechanism functions, riddles & puzzles, flavor texts, back story, affiliations, anthropological and sociological elements, etc. It's not about writing out every word that can be said, it's about fleshing out as much of the reality as possible that'll be around when SOMETHING gets said.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nAsIf \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Why is rolling dice and consulting a table fairer than making stuff up?
      This is a blurry line, so take it with a grain of salt, but I see different degrees of applicability depending on the type of question.

      For empirical questions of a stochastic nature, like "Is it raining today?" or "Who is the first person I see at the market?" it makes perfect sense to roll these things, even in a "No Myth" experience. It represents the uncertainty we have here in Reality, about real rain, and the unpredictability of people moving around within a city - these are both large chaotic systems, and yet they do remain within heuristic boundaries - which are represented by the table itself. The dice represent the chaotic and unpredictable forces of Reality, and the table represents the possible ways this particular piece of reality might go (i.e. before we open Schrodinger's Box).

      Under these circumstances, the dice represent the complex and capricious nature of Reality, which is larger than the GM.

      Your argument is that random things are random, but I don't see an argument that random things are more fair than making stuff up.

      Where's the /unfairness/ in making stuff up?

      \n
      \n In reference to the original post...
      I think the point lays in mental perception and what different people find enjoyable.

      If you KNOW that the GM is deciding everything on the fly, you can not be clever, you can not be lucky, whatever you do is a concession from the GM. You feel little agency.
      In a GM-Less system this feeling might still stand, as you don't recognize your fellow co-Players any special authority... they are still "a person" deciding what happens on a whim.

      If the GM is doing the same exact thing but you DON'T KNOW IT and instead you think it is all pre-written somewhere... then whatever happens is by your merit or fault; there IS an "objective reality" (as far as the notes go) thus a better way to do things, and you found it... implicitly you solve a puzzle. Or if you fail, there is some sort of plausible/causal reason.

      Randomness falls in between because even though it is not "objective" it is still "fate".
      You live or die not by the whim and concession of another person, but because of a mechanical, neutral, external, FAIR entity.
      You can even rationalize that you chose to take a risk, which is still a meaningful action.

      The end result is always the same.
      But the perception of it is very different.

      \n Adam, maybe it's not more "fair" but it feels more "fair" subjectively from a Simulationist perspective. Not defending any point of view here, just making an observation. Note the grain of salt! But I think the concept of "fairness" has both objective and subjective qualities.

      ETA: This is one of those areas where GMing blurs into game design. How "accurate" are those tables? That's part of the question too (at least from a Sim perspective).

      \n Given the focus on prep of place versus prep of plot, I have to ask if Dungeon World's Fronts and maps give you enough of a "Full Myth" experience. Does the map have to be completely detailed, or is a rough sketch enough? Does leaving blanks hurt?

      Or is this a sliding scale thing, where ideally the DM would have Castle Greyhawk all planned out (but not any kind of plot, just what and where things were to start with) and everyone would explore it, while understanding that that level of detail is tough to get in practice?

      I'm trying to get a sense for where you personally draw the line, so I can figure out what kind of techniques might help while staying on the Full-Myth/Full-Prep side of your personal line.

      I personally lean more towards the some-prep but not full-prep side of things. That is one reason why I seized on things like Fronts, and Diaspora's social conflicts, and Mouse Guard's mission structure is that it lets me plan out an overall structure while letting me improvise moment-to-moment stuff. I try to improvise everything as a sole GM and it gets too loose. (Fiasco or Microscope are different, particularly because you can revisit things fractally in Microscope.)

      The flip side of this is that I also use a lot of reincorporation; and I don't change stuff once it's established, even when I'm the only one who knows what it was. ("Always say what your prep demands" in Apocalypse World parlance). I had a villain once who got offed mid-arc by the players because of the properties of a magical artifact he had.

      \n Dave,

      Awesome observations on the practice of this kind of thing. I always love hearing how methodically you've observed the details of this kind of stuff with your gaming group; that kind of insight is very rare. I'd love to hear more about it, in fact, but I don't have a specific question right now ("it" = your list of what kind of GM input/technique is acceptable, and which crosses the line).

      phoenix182,

      You make lots of good points about the subjectivity of the whole thing, and how much it can rely on personal preference. \n

      What I was trying to say is that within a given subjective preference of outcomes one method will achieve greater degrees of success more consistently...at least in my experience. For people that like the things I talked about my tables enjoying they're going to be achieved more often with careful, detailed prep than with improvisation (for the reasons I touched on before).
      \nI still take issue with this, however. First of all, improvisation CAN be trained to a very high level; that just rarely happens in the modern Western world. (There used to be trained improvisational storytellers as well as similar musical traditions in various cultures, but we rarely see that these days, with the exception of jazz, dance-offs, and freestyle rap.)

      I've definitely seen gaming groups create incredible stuff without it being pre-plotted. (c.f. All Narrativist gaming, ever. Some LARP traditions. GMless play. Online roleplaying like forum play. And so on.) Absolutely, to the extent that it surpasses a lot or most typical "plotted" play.

      (I recognize that this is very difficult to believe if you haven't experienced it first-hand. As one example, however, I can point to musical theorists, who have started analyzing high-level improvised music - by true masters, mind you - and finding all the same organizational elements, like thematic development, pitch class set use, motivic pairing, successful Schenkerian analysis, and so forth, that they find in great classical symphonies or similar works.)

      Just like debate about other aspects of subjective taste (love, music, food, whatever), the best way to appreciate this is to consider why you might reject a more extreme position than your own.

      Consider this as an example: if deciding things in advance makes the experience so much better, why not have the players play from a script, too?

      Well, you probably value the activity of spontaneous dialogue, right? I doubt very much that the dialogue at your table will be delivered as "well" by your players (unless they are professional actors, perhaps) when they are reading from a script. Dialogue will likely feel stilted and unnatural, instead.

      It's an example of how with each added layer of preparation, some other valued feature disappears or is diminished.

      So, if we decide dialogue must remain "fresh", what else? Surely you could prepare even better as a GM if you could first have the players tell you exactly how they plan to react to certain events or circumstances? Let's say a player tells you that their character hates Klingorians so much that they would erupt into violence when they see one. Now you can introduce a Klingorian at the exact moment where you want violence to break out.

      That would allow you to create an even more finely-crafted experience.

      But surely it's not hard to see how some other, highly valuable features of play would go by the wayside. In this case, it's the player's ability to engage in spontaneous creative (or strategic) choices.

      In the same way, limiting the GM's responses reduces some of their creative input into the game (even if it's from the GM's own notes and planning).

      I'm not arguing for any particular point along that spectrum: I think it's very worthwhile to explore the full range of planned to spontaneous.

      I just want to put in my two cents on a topic which I find fascinating, and take a rhetorical stand against the idea that a certain technique is unconditionally superior.

      \n Eero,

      Your last post is excellent.\n

      [...] in reality Full Myth runs into some drastic difficulties if one tries to take it as some sort of an absolute guideline. It's not a value in itself, one might say.
      \nPrecisely. It's about the "how" and the "when", not just the "how much".\n

      There is no contradiction to having the same game feature high and low arbitrariness at different times, because No Myth vs. Full Myth is only a continuum in the abstract; in reality we have various stages of the game that are set up in different ways, and prepped for with different techniques, and we can thus only talk about the Myth level in the aggregate. As Sandra describes, of course she doesn't mean by "Full Myth" that she tries to simulate everything - nobody could do that and run an interesting game at the same time. It's just a matter of relative emphasis, whether you've got more Myth than some other game might.
      \nSince we've all been discussing Forge theory for a bit, I'd like to mention that THIS is where I personally find the concept of Creative Agenda immensely helpful. (Hopefully this is interesting to you, Sandra.)

      It allows me - as a player, as a designer, in any role - to cut through a lot of typical roleplaying debates and do what is going to make the game the most fun it can be.

      Typical RPG debates might go on about how locations have to be prepped, whether things should always be rolled for or not, and so forth. In other words, we're talking about Technical Agenda, personal preferences for techniques and mechanics, and so forth.

      However, a game group that has a strong sense of the creative goals of their game doesn't NEED any of that. (Not that they must be *intellectually* aware of Forge-mandated Creative Agenda or anything like that - of course it can be done "naively". This is not an argument for theory on my part.)

      Eero's "challenge-based adventuring" model can effortlessly jump from prepped modules to random tables to whim-based creativity, from "letting the dice fall where they may" to a sort of metagame protection (of course we should let the drunken sailor's player a fair chance to escape his captors) and even dramatic coordination (we don't need to establish via dice rolls whether an adventure hook exists in the particular area where the characters are hanging out - it doesn't harm us in any way to agree as a group that they do or do not).

      This, to me, is the real payoff of the awareness of creative agenda. When we are focused on the creative goals of the game, we can use the tools we need in the places where we need them, instead of getting bogged down in endless debate about the tools themselves.

      In this particular example, we certainly couldn't reverse the tools: have the GM decide on a whim when characters live or die, but roll dice to establish which strategies the characters are allowed to use in this particular session, and so on.

      An understanding of the creative agenda of a particular game allows us to stop bickering about technical matters out of context, and, instead, find ever better ways to apply those technical tools to the game at hand.

      That's where, for me, this stuff delivers real-world, dramatic, practical functionality.

      In this case, is Full Myth better? It really depends. What's this game about?

      Maybe, depending on your priorities, you'll find that having detailed character backgrounds is really important, but establishing local customs can be done on-the-fly, because one is key to what we're trying to explore in this game... and the other is just dressing.

      "Fair" here just means "the way that makes me happy," whatever that is. It's a totally indescribable emotion without rational explanation?
      I feel ya, but it's a bit more than that. It's related to the notions of when and how much, as discussed above by Eero and Paul. And "accuracy" or verisimilitude (of a fictional place!)

      Remember I'm talking about questions which are empirical and represent stochastic samplings of complex systems (we can talk about other sorts of questions later). To a Full Myth Simulationist, which I am sometimes, this sort of table is a dynamic system which effectively is (represents the effects of) an actual system in the world. A classic example would be a subsystem that determines local weather based on season and regional climate.

      You might say the table itself (its internal logic, along with any rules or variables attached) can be a diegetic prop rather than a non-diegetic tool. This is qualitatively different than (say) rolling Rory's Story cubes and stringing the results together.
