      \n Lots and lots of answers when I was asleep!\n
      It's easier to referee [new emphasis] fairly if you give up some amount of control to your co-DM, the dice!
      \nWhy is rolling dice and consulting a table fairer than making stuff up?
      \nWhen I'm running the orcs (making their decisions) and deciding their strength and number at the same time, what am I? Am I adversary, am I referee, am I a careful bird that has to beat up the players very very gently?
      When the amount of orcs and their strength has been pre-set on the map, and the players run into them, and I have to run the orcs and fight for them, what am I? A referee that should focus on portraying the world vividly (which includes having the orcs fight fiercely).
      The more time that passes between me placing those orcs vs me running those orcs, the easier the compartmentalization process is. I don't get my "hats" jumbled up".
      If someone else (a module writer or a stocking algorithm writer) helped me place those orcs, so much the better!

      When "filling the holes" (i.e. "gosh darn it the players rob an orc store and the orc owner called the orc police, I need an unplanned squad of orcs pronto"), going to the dice to determine their number and strength is, in my thinking, more aligned with my current role as DM, which is portraying the word vividly.\n

      Actually, having a full myth sense of place, faction mechanics a la SWN etc are not necessarily in the way of players improvizing and jazzing with their character's actions.
      I'm not talking about "Hey let's run Rise of the Railroads from start to finish I hear there's an awesome giant fight at the end!". I'm talking about players being free to move about and interact with each other and the objects and monsters in an imagined [past tense] world.

      Most of the cool things that happened in no myth games can still happen in full myth games but now with the added resonance of "and we were here".

      \nHere are my answers to the Telephone GM.

      The reason I rail against the plotting is because the introduction of that to this debate leads to a lot of side tracks to what I'm the most curious about. I mean we both love the jazz. That's just the facts. You can jazz even with prep. You don't say "Anakin will say 'Darth, I am your father!' when Darth is dangling over the air duct pipes on Cloud City", you write down "BTW, Anakin is Darth's father" on a character sheet and then see what happens.
      I'm a big fan of excluding the middle (the so-called "zone of suck") but in this case the middle is the gold for me. The "prepared scene" is boring [to me] and the "improvised paternal relationship" is boring [to me] compared to the "holy shit this guy is gonna be this guy's dad but they don't know about it".

      \n Oh, I agree. My comments about the plotting were in response to phoenix182, who claimed (initially) that *prepared* material was always better than *improvised* material (or had the potential to be).

      You don't see a contradiction between the desire to define things beforehand and, at the same time, being excited about rolling the dice to create surprise?

      As for the telephone GM thing: your answers suggest that it's not about *improvising* or *preparing*, but just about the hygiene of the process (in Eero's terms).

      You want to be sure that the GM isn't altering the reality of things in response to character action, and (for various reasons) you do not trust the GM to be capable of doing this through mental discipline.

      I've often used this little device:

      Players: "Ok, the mirror is in the middle of the room? Then we want to..."
      GM: "Wait! Before you say what, I need to nail down some details about the mirror... [scribble scribble, and/or roll dice] OK, go ahead! What do you do?"

      I use that kind of thing when I don't trust my own mental discipline.

      \n Trust in them and the dice are magical; they know exactly what to say. That's true in all games, myth or no myth! :-)

      In a Full Myth campaign, for me, the tables and dice serve as an intermediary between pure improv and prepped or semi-prepped elements. I don't necessarily see a contradiction there either, although if I'm to consider it "Full Myth" it requires a certain level of logic and breadth in the construction of those tables. Knowing the logic of the table that represents monsters in this region, or weather on this island, allows me to know my world - even though I don't roll the dice for this particular day until we get to that particular spot.

      In No-Myth games I'll do binary trees (50-50 yes/no chains) at the drop of a hat, starting from "the most obvious thing that occurs to me at this moment" - even for major details. But that doesn't feel like knowing my world; it feels like discovering random stuff. In a Full Myth campaign I only use that technique for tertiary details.

      I like both types of games, because different games are aiming for different things. There's a different kind of internal reward that I feel when comparing these approaches subjectively. I'd even hazard a guess that they release different neurotransmitters.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n In that case, I actually don't think it's a spectrum, but a binary.

      It doesn't matter how far ahead of time it was prepared. What matters is simply: was this "thing" invented and/or defined in response to a specific action on the part of the characters ("we tip the mirror over!"), or not (i.e. before the characters interacted with it)?

      (Certainly, gray areas are possible - "OK, the characters are in the room with it, they'll probably look into it, right?" or "Even before the campaign started, I knew a mirror would be cool, because Jeff likes to make characters who are narcissists and like to check themselves out in mirrors." - but, in theory, there is a line somewhere.)

      Next question:

      Do you realize that, no matter what you come up with, no matter how well-defined, there will still be moments where you have to improvise? You might know how the mirror works, but then a player says, "Ok, I pour blood on it, so everything reflected in it turns red. What happens NOW?" And of course you never thought of that, so you've got to come up with something.

      That's why prep is good, but principled GMing and mental hygiene/discipline are arguably more important (or, at least, they're STILL important). And then - the most important ingredient - a sense of trust.
      \n

      In that case, I actually don't think it's a spectrum, but a binary.
      \nYes, if it turns out to be true that that is the awesome part.
      I don't know... there's just something about a "full world" that seems to be missing in the telephone co-GM / "let me scribble down the mirror" case but I can't put my finger on it.\n

      But yes, I've realized that from the start. That's why you have a referee instead of just playing Zork. And it's very problematic from the hygiene/compartmentalization angle.

      If the angle in the thread is "How can we convince Sandra to go back to No Myth playing?", I don't think that's going to go very far. But by all means keep trying.

      However, the angle I had was neither of those. It was "Why is Full Myth so awesome and how can I get there consistently and easily"?

      \n I understand the need to separate place-prep from story-prep, but we know that all the best places have a few stories of their own and that a lot of adventures are about connecting the dots to find them and play your role within them. If you think of a place as a special kind of NPC, it's easy to see the risk of it being more important than the PCs.

      But these are all generic terms, maybe it's clearer if we talk about specific games. In something like Dungeon Crawl Classics, I can totally see the fun in having a fully-prepared map with all kinds of levers and knobs that the poor level 0 characters can mess with. On the other hand, in something like Call of Cthulhu, I'm not sure about situations where, for example, the investigators connect the dots and pool their income to hire a bunch of world-war veterans to storm the hiding place of the local cultists. At that point, I think that the solvable "map" has become too big for me.

      \n

      Eero's "challenge-based adventuring" model can effortlessly jump from prepped modules to random tables to whim-based creativity, from "letting the dice fall where they may" to a sort of metagame protection (of course we should let the drunken sailor's player a fair chance to escape his captors) and even dramatic coordination (we don't need to establish via dice rolls whether an adventure hook exists in the particular area where the characters are hanging out - it doesn't harm us in any way to agree as a group that they do or do not).
      \nAs a sidebar, this struck me as a perfect description of why the "Eero Game" model of OSR play appeals to me. The ability to technically resolve things in a way that shifts in response to the needs of the situation strikes me as Design Holy Grail territory.

      It is, to return to the topic at hand, the style I'd advocate for finding and easily returning to (the feel, if not the body of) Full Myth.

      Isn't it usually in heroic fantasy? Get the treasure, fulfill the prophecy, slay the monster, rescue the princess, save the city, etc.
      \nI rolled up a couple of city encounters with Red Tide's tables (but set them in Zakhara, al-Qadim). A merchant house that were going through a generational shift, and being plagued by a thief.
      The players upon showing up with their characters went "OK we are thieves and urchins and my [one of the player's characters] life goal is to become the greatest thief that ever lived!". The thief that plagued the merchant houses became their NPC sidekick!

      In our B4 The Lost City, the players joined up with one of the factions and waged war against another of the factions.

      In our Caverns of Thracia, one PC died in the first room then the party never returned.

      \n\nAirk \n\n\n

      \n
      \n
      It's "play to find out", not "play to make it up". That's also part of the adoration of the dice. They surprise you.
      \nIrrelevant. Even if the GM makes everything up, he is still playing to find out as long as the players also have meaningful input into the game. This goes back to what Ben Robbins had to say on the difference between randomness (Dice rolling) and uncertainty (Not knowing what's going to happen because there are other people at the table and you can't predict them). While randomness is one way to generate uncertainty, you don't need randomness to have uncertainty, and uncertainty is where the interesting stuff comes from.

      I also think reframing the discussion as "prep" just makes my point more clear - it doesn't matter if the prep was done 30 seconds ago or 30 years ago. All that matters is that it was done before the prep is interacted with. The fact that you attempted (and apparently, from what I can tell, felt you got bad results from) to play a game where everything was made up in the exact second it was touched doesn't mean that's the only way to do No Myth. In fact, that strikes me as a way to catastrophically fail at running a No Myth game, and I find it baffling that you insist on holding it up as your "This didn't work for me, so I've gone to completely the other end of the spectrum" example. This has enormous downsides with no upside that I can discern:

      -- It creates extra work for yourself because instead of being able to think "Okay, there's a bomb on the table, what does a bomb look like?" you have to think "Okay, there's a thing on the table, and it looks like... a box? And it's uhm, 12cm by 20cm. And there are some wires? Maybe? And it might be a bomb...but I can't decide that yet..."
      -- It suffers from all the second guessing "well, now that they've picked this hallway, am I a jerk for putting a trap in it? But if I don't put a trap in it, they'll never run into any traps..." stuff that you raise as concern in this thread.
      -- It makes it enormously difficult to keep things coherent.
      -- You have to deliberately work to not make up stuff in advance, which is, as best as I can tell, contrary to human nature. It's super weird to say "There's a hallway" and then not think, in even the vaguest of terms, about where it might go.

      But virtually all of this trouble gets swept out of the way if you allow the GM to think ahead. Which is still No Myth play.\n
      However, the angle I had was neither of those. It was "Why is Full Myth so awesome and how can I get there consistently and easily"?
      \nThat's kindof the whole point. You CAN'T get there "easily". This is the reason people are essentially telling you "Hey, you can get 95% of the same effect for 25% of the work, so you might want to think about how to do that well." As best as I can tell from my limited perspective, "No myth" began because "full myth" was way too bloody much work for the reward. Full myth is the stuff of "I have this massive 3 ring binder full of Campaign notes, but the stuff my PCs have encountered fits on three pages. Oh, and we've been playing for two years." It's the same school as "I wrote up twelve pages of backstory for my character, and none of it ever comes out in play." It requires enormous amounts of work because you have to try to prepare EVERYTHING, or you're breaking your own myth and it all becomes a lie. Except it's always going to be a lie. There's always going to be some amount of stuff that you just had to make up. So there is no "full myth" or "complete prep" or anything.

      So I guess I too am interested in the answer for "Why 2097 thinks Full Myth is so awesome" in light of the fact that you really haven't posted anything here that is dependent on Full Myth at all. It just seems more like you had problems with your attempts at No Myth, and now you are swinging the pendulum back the other way as firmly as possible.

      The fact that you attempted (and apparently, from what I can tell, felt you got bad results from) to play a game where everything was made up in the exact second it was touched doesn't mean that's the only way to do No Myth. In fact, that strikes me as a way to catastrophically fail at running a No Myth game, and I find it baffling that you insist on holding it up as your "This didn't work for me, so I've gone to completely the other end of the spectrum" example. This has enormous downsides with no upside that I can discern:
      It creates extra work for yourself because instead of being able to think "Okay, there's a bomb on the table, what does a bomb look like?" you have to think "Okay, there's a thing on the table, and it looks like... a box? And it's uhm, 12cm by 20cm. And there are some wires? Maybe? And it might be a bomb...but I can't decide that yet..."

      That's not how it sounds. I can just spout it off. It'll be inconsistent. It'll be gonzo. It'll be flawed. I'll have poor player retention rates. But I can just say "It's a box, 12 by 20 cm. There's two green wires and a stripped one where the copper glints under the flickering bulb." It just comes.
      That's why I was attracted to No Myth.\n
      It suffers from all the second guessing "well, now that they've picked this hallway, am I a jerk for putting a trap in it? But if I don't put a trap in it, they'll never run into any traps..." stuff that you raise as concern in this thread.
      \nYes, this sucked.\n
      You have to deliberately work to not make up stuff in advance, which is, as best as I can tell, contrary to human nature. It's super weird to say "There's a hallway" and then not think, in even the vaguest of terms, about where it might go.
      \nSometimes I had ideas of scenes and lines come to me... and they usually did somehow or another end up in the game, often climactically. That's part of why I think my extreme No Myth style led to lessened player argency. It's GM's dreamland.\n
      But virtually all of this trouble gets swept out of the way if you allow the GM to think ahead. Which is still No Myth play.
      \nIt's the excluded "middle". And in this thread we're sussing out where the line goes, what's awesome and what's not awesome.
      When I'm speaking of mythness, I'm thinking of a line between "absolutely ready for anything" to "making everything up in that very second". I was pretty damn close to that latter pole. I'm aiming for somewhere in between.
      Say something like most OSR games... like the Sine Nomine Games for example.
      Have a few dungeons prepared, a couple of wilderness tables, a little more.
      I've been running 30 pretty damn good sessions of D&D this last year.\n
      That's kindof the whole point. You CAN'T get there "easily". This is the reason people are essentially telling you "Hey, you can get 95% of the same effect for 25% of the work, so you might want to think about how to do that well." As best as I can tell from my limited perspective, "No myth" began because "full myth" was way too bloody much work for the reward. Full myth is the stuff of "I have this massive 3 ring binder full of Campaign notes, but the stuff my PCs have encountered fits on three pages. Oh, and we've been playing for two years." It's the same school as "I wrote up twelve pages of backstory for my character, and none of it ever comes out in play." It requires enormous amounts of work because you have to try to prepare EVERYTHING, or you're breaking your own myth and it all becomes a lie. Except it's always going to be a lie. There's always going to be some amount of stuff that you just had to make up. So there is no "full myth" or "complete prep" or anything.
      \nBut again, small things... I'm fine with giving things a little color. Someone asks "what color is the wallpaper" and there's not a wallpaper-color-puzzle? I'll make it up! Someone asks "what color is the wallpaper" and there's a blue-eating gaki that's chasing them? Too bad, I guess I'll have to roll for it!
      I am aiming for living in the real world. It's just that I want "more myth" than I've had.\n
      So I guess I too am interested in the answer for "Why 2097 thinks Full Myth is so awesome" in light of the fact that you really haven't posted anything here that is dependent on Full Myth at all. It just seems more like you had problems with your attempts at No Myth, and now you are swinging the pendulum back the other way as firmly as possible.
      \nSure. But I'm trying to have an open mind while discussing it.

      \nThis actually helps me understand where you're coming from a bit better. Part of what I think is going on for me in this thread is figuring out what you value, since that's the central question here and it's different from what I value, so I can't easily model it in my head without all these questions.

      In that vein, another thought experiment: You're running a West-Marches-style thing, where the players handle the scheduling and then tell you where they plan to go. You use this information to know what you need to prep ahead of time. You stick to the planned setting as much as possible, but with no expectation as to the outcome of the players' meddling. Where does this fall in your personal sense? Just fine? Works-but-could-be-better? Doesn't work?

      \n@Adam_Dray, I'd say that dice are more fair than GMs for social reasons. The exact same impulses which allow us to work together as humans at the game table (picking up on social cues, getting caught up in the fun of the moment, etc.) also serve to bias us against consistency and objectivity. A perfectly consistent and objective GM would be just as fair as dice, but that person does not exist. And even if they do, no players would believe in them.

      Now, if the group doesn't define fairness that way, or only cares about fairness up to a certain point, then there's no issue with the GM just deciding. So I think the points you've been making apply to most gaming. Just not all.

      \n\nphoenix182 \n\n\n

      \n
      Dave,

      I still take issue with this, however. First of all, improvisation CAN be trained to a very high level; that just rarely happens in the modern Western world. (There used to be trained improvisational storytellers as well as similar musical traditions in various cultures, but we rarely see that these days, with the exception of jazz, dance-offs, and freestyle rap.)

      \nI have no question that it might be possible (almost everything is), just in our experience it isn't practically likely and therefore not worth significant consideration. After all, can you point to a Billboard top 100 that features freestyle rap? Some songs might have started as freestyle, but then they were likely heavily modified and polished to be an official release. Not that Billboard is the sole measure of what's good, but you get the point.

      Players do script. Not only do my players create their characters, but they're welcome to join in world creation (though we do it during preparation far more than at play). They also provide plot and storylines (which I will then usually embellish or tool to fit well in the big picture).

      I also know (in general) how they'll react. I know their character alignments (or equivalent), their charcter backgrounds, the game setting information, and I know their tendencies as players. While no DM will ever prepare for everything they'll do, the better you prep the less frequently they'll surprise you, and the deeper and more intricate the games will become.

      Yes the content of dialogue generally remains fluid, but often the topic, bullet points, and mannerisms of dialogue are scripted, just as with published adventures. That's what allows the depth and breadth, which would otherwise be nearly impossible to do well on the fly.

      Prep in no way limits creativity or improvisational response (after all, prep IS creativity and improvisation...just done before the game begins). It merely brings the imaginary world closer to reality (or in this case, good fiction) thereby improving immersion and verisimilitude, allowing for the dynamic aspects to flow freer and richer. It's the difference between Temple of Apshai, Ultima II, or Wizardry and Witcher III or Skyrim. It just makes the same gaming experience richer and prettier - IF you value the specific aspects that are changed over the ones that aren't at least.
      \n

      Oh, I agree. My comments about the plotting were in response to phoenix182, who claimed (initially) that *prepared* material was always better than *improvised* material (or had the potential to be).

      \nI want to highlight that I never meant to say that...remember I was saying only that preparation allows more depth and breadth...more details, better refined language, more technically accurate potential, the potential for prop creation (or miniaturization of the setting), better inter-connectivity in setting and plot points, more diabolical traps, more flavor fluff, etc. Which only matters if those things matter to the people at your table.

      \n\nWarriorMonk \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      I want to highlight that I never meant to say that...remember I was saying only that preparation allows more depth and breadth...more details, better refined language, more technically accurate potential, the potential for prop creation (or miniaturization of the setting), better inter-connectivity in setting and plot points, more diabolical traps, more flavor fluff, etc. Which only matters if those things matter to the people at your table.
      \nI supported phoenix because of that, but my post got buried under that and looks like only the use of the ill-word got any attention. Ok, forget the ill-word part, I had two points there that have been argumented a lot ever since:

      -Learning to improvise is another way of doing prep.

      -From actual experience, I back phoenix182 in that doing as much prep as you can allows you to give the players good game experience on almost each session, versus the fact that going no-myth may give you an even better experience, but not as often as going full-myth will do... if your players are into that, of course.

      Why do we still endorse the use of (at least a bit of) prepared material over going no-myth impro? At some point, both are produced in exactly the same way. Either the GM or the players come up with an idea and both help to connect that idea with the shared fiction. But then, the prepared material is polished: a short NPC monologue can be revised for a better effect; a random monster can become a metaphor, a mysterious murder can be better linked with clues and witnesses; proper red herrings can be added. A place or a creature could be drawn instead of just described and a piece of music to give the proper ambience to a particular scene can be searched with more care and less waste of game time.

      And the GM may notice in time that the original idea may ruin some aspect of the fictional space, stop in her tracks and rethink the whole thing.


      But it's not for nothing that I wrote above that going no-myth may give you an even better experience. There are times when the game attains flow by itself, everybody's inspiration is on fire and magical moments come by itself better than if they were pre-written by geniuses. Certainly the GM and players can train themselves to achieve this more consistently, yet the miracle of perfect improvisation is certain to happen less often.

    Why is Full Myth so awesome? I don't get it

      @Adam_Dray, I'd say that dice are more fair than GMs for social reasons. The exact same impulses which allow us to work together as humans at the game table (picking up on social cues, getting caught up in the fun of the moment, etc.) also serve to bias us against consistency and objectivity. A perfectly consistent and objective GM would be just as fair as dice, but that person does not exist. And even if they do, no players would believe in them.

      Now, if the group doesn't define fairness that way, or only cares about fairness up to a certain point, then there's no issue with the GM just deciding. So I think the points you've been making apply to most gaming. Just not all.
      \nI get it. I understand that "fair" here is saying, "Hey, we all can agree to use this third party, this impartial referee, a fair die roll on this random table, whenever I haven't explicitly defined what is there beforehand."

      I don't define fairness that way. A randomly chosen result that doesn't make sense for a game is less fair, to me, than a purposefully result made-up by the GM that does make sense for the game.

      Or maybe I'm saying that it might be fair, but to me it isn't /good/. Other people want that impartiality.

      When I'm GMing, I tend to be No Myth. I make most of it up on the fly. When I'm at my best, it comes out of a deep part of my subconscious, even to the point that it surprises me sometimes, or feels like I'm discovering it rather than inventing it. My wife (a traditionally published author) says similar things about her writing process, when she has "flow" moments, how characters write their own dialog.

      Of course, I have to apply "hygenic" techniques to make sure I'm not forcing the players around. Because I generally loathe such play, I am pretty good about an internal sense of "fairness" and I tend to choose things for a certain kind of game using a "what is the most likely thing to be here? what is the most interesting thing to be here?" set of measuring sticks.

      For me, I think I'm getting that same feeling of discovery from watching my subconscious invent things as y'all get from rolling on a random table.

      \n For human GMs, unable to grasp exactly how much is enough realism/objectivity, but conscious enough to worry about trying, I believe that "fairness" is a dynamic thing in the sense that they will sometimes favor the players, others favor the dice and some other times come up with something for the sake of the story, in a way that the whole will favor the game experience.

      \n I think the key is in the mental process. Why are you even worried about fair? Try to judge what represents the "reality" best. Let's say the group is ambushed by a guard patrol. Well, how big are guard patrols in this time and place? You can totally enlist the players' help in figuring it out. It could be a number or it could be a range, which is then rolled.

      But now it's set: we know a little more about the world. Now the GM and the players both know what to expect from a typical guard patrol.

      \n
      \n Ok, what about a full myth where:

      -Random tables are created with the players before play and open to their scrutiny at any moment.
      -Setting locations are created with the players on a brainstorm on the first session.
      -GM details everything within a "budget limit" with the aid of the random tables. Budget limit helps the GM indicate the players the real CR of the encounter.
      -Map contains encounters and obstacles that might be of a higher level than the party, but they know that from before (through rumours and clues in their way there) so they can decide if they venture into that place anyway or not.
      -If the GM doesn't know/ain't sure/still hasn't come up with something, she asks the players. If the GM finds the players idea troublesome to include, she states so or let them roll the dice to see if what they say is the exact true or if they are not well informed (but the truth still resembles what they said in some form)

      Sorry, I'm still shooting a bit in the darkness with those ones, but I've got the feeling that the thrill of full myth you're looking for is a bit like chess, Greek Tragedy and Mc Gyver. in the sense that the fiction is in a fixed state before players reach the place (chess in the sense that both sides start on equal terms but one of them does the first move), so players can get there and try whatever they want (Mc Gyver).

      Part of the thrill for the GM comes from her knowing exactly what the players will face (greek tragedy; the audience knows this will end bad but still watches until the end) but then being surprised that they take the elements and change the story into something completely different through wits or brawl. (Mc Gyver again)

      As in chess there's the thrill of the intelectual challenge: who outwits who with the same elements; but it won't work if the challenge isn't in equal terms. In this paradigm making things on the spot gives the GM an unfair advantage or just plainly kills the thrill.

      I'm probably still off of what you want for a mile but I think I get now that what you want and what we've been offering are widely different things because all GMs get their kicks from different things and full-myth and no-myth don't define nor get us any close to label what we actually like about doing prep or improvising on the spot.

      \n
      \n In case my replies thus far are hopelessly incoherent from appearing out of order, here's a more precise rephrase of the stuff that sounded controversial in my first draft:

      1) The players are happy when I look up fictional facts.

      2) The players are happy when I roll to evaluate in-fiction probabilities (does it rain today?), as they're well-modeled (don't forget, we're in a low-rain terrain).

      3) The players are happy when I state with complete certainty what happens without any look-ups or rolls. This gives the impression that there is in fact a true reality and correct answer and I already know it. I am a qualified expert on the real place that is the game world.

      4) The players will grow unhappy with any of these methods which shows any pattern of producing implausible nonsense. "Simple statement" is on the shortest leash; most of my players are quicker to question a human than a table or written-down info.

      5) When I state something with complete certainty, the players do not care about the process leading up to my statement. However, if I state something without complete certainty, the players dislike having my process revealed to them. There are various ways to hide my own uncertainty and thus keep the players happy, such as filling the time I take to think with habitual tasks like die-rolling or paper-flipping. Having discussed this out of game, the players have confirmed that they approve of my illusions as long as they're successful. Things like far-away glances which betray creative thought give the illusion away and are thus unwelcome.

      6) The players are happy to be asked for established fictional info, as long as I can seamlessly fall back to an appropriate Plan B if they don't remember the info. If they don't remember whether the info was ever established, I can look-up or roll. If they remember the info was established but not what it was, I must look-up.

      7) I do not recall any methods not listed above that the players are also happy with. They are not happy with any methods that transparently invent the game environment on the spot.
      \n

      \n Instead of linking to the whole post, I should have just re-pasted the relevant part of my earlier post:\n
      Why (this type of) Full Myth is so awesome:
      The advantage to playing this way is hard to explain, but concerns the weight of the fictional world and the realness of the events within, both during play and after play. It's perhaps less important to in-the-moment fiction-based satisfaction than it is to a sense of real-world permanence and achievement over time. In imprecise shorthand: "There's a big difference between changing (through roleplaying in it) a world that comes and goes and changing a world that's solid and persistent."

      To me this still doesn't feel awesome. That's not just from the sound of it, I have AP experience that is similar.
      This was a worthwhile tangent -- if it hadn't been for my emo outburst -- but it doesn't strike me as close to the core of the awesomeness of myth.

      Among the components of a good, mythfull presentation, it keeps the least important ones and discards the most important ones. The ones it does keep (confidence, well-modellness, looking things up, consistency) are good. The ones it does discard (as of yet unknown qualities) are, however, much more crucial.

      \n Allow me to meddle a bit more, I get both your points, perhaps this will help a bit.

      For both David and me, a big part of being fair is more like "Karma", as in, if a player is nice to the world (roleplays, describes, gives input, asks before breaking something for fun) then the world must be nice to the player and is only then that we tend to "cheat" and make up things in the spot.

      David, for Sandra being fair is not making up things nor allowing karma to meddle in her judgement. She's got less problems than us with that because the players in her group don't need as much incentives as ours to "be nice to the world". That's why our ill-word tricks are absolutely out of the question for her.

      \n That's nice to hear Sandra, David and I had to come up with mechanics to deal with some of our players bad habits, and even today I can't help but design thinking in what can go wrong :P but back to the thread, I think it reached a good point right before all this derrailing.

      \n
      To me this still doesn't feel awesome . . . Among the components of a good, mythfull presentation, it keeps the least important ones and discards the most important ones. The ones it does keep (confidence, well-modellness, looking things up, consistency) are good. The ones it does discard (as of yet unknown qualities) are, however, much more crucial.
      Interesting! Well, just to clarify my own experience, I'm not one of the folks who values good modeling and consistency as ends in themselves -- for me, they're always means to an end. And in this case, the thing they produce -- a gameworld that feels like a place that really exists -- isn't even the ultimate point. The ultimate points are out in the real world, when players in New York chat with players in North Carolina about different experiences in a place they've both shared, where one party's efforts are discovered by another party years (of real time, not game time) later. It's not that the NY-NC chats happen often, or for most players; that's just a dramatic example. Less-dramatic examples include remembering a game far longer than other games, chatting with fellow players about last session as if it was something you lived through together (as opposed to being like a board game you played or a movie you watched), or just being proud to have had an inn named after your character, moreso than in another game.

      I won't try to sell you on this; if it doesn't appeal to you, then fair enough. I just wanted to try to make clear why I love it so much.

      Any thoughts on your more crucial unknown qualities? If I've missed them somewhere in the sea of prior posts, I apologize.

      \n That NY-NC exchange does sound absolutely awesome. West Marches style. I have had some similar more local moments (since players come and go). I do try my best to get some consistency and good modeling in, with the help of the players of course.

      But for crucial qualities -- how about that mirror story again?
      The fact that the module writer put that in there and the fact that we engaged with it like an ever-lovin' guerilla team. We were in that dungeon building barricades, tearing down walls, creating extra exits, ambushing scorpions, pouring glue on skeletons... (Now Eero is gonna come and say it's Gam. But I'm over GNS and even over GDNS Official, Canonical at the moment! It's all about technical agenda, not about creative agenda!)

      \n
      \n My first reaction is to say the Full Myth benefit there is a well-engineered interactive scenario, and that it's hard to improvise those. It's not hard to improvise flavor and imagery (for me, anyway), but it's hard to improvise a situation that's neither (a) impossible nor (b) has one obvious right solution nor (c) is a piece of cake if the players show any competence.

      What do you think? Is that on track, or still missing the crucial bits?

      If it's on track, I do have a thought about how No Myth sandbox trial and error can produce a similar thing over time via players skipping all the scenarios that aren't awesome... but that may rely on a play culture that's hard to pass along.

      \n Some folks use modules for darts -- cheap on GM effort, but not on cash!

      Good situation/scenario design is one of those practical gaps in RPG theory I was complaining about in another thread. I can think of a few examples from games I like for full-prep options, but not for improv. Actually, I wonder... where does Dogs in the Vineyard town creation fall? There's kind of a Myth in the skeleton of who's committed which sins against whom, but then the GM can improv a lot of the other specifics if they want... I can talk about the process, but I'm not seeing much use in the Full Myth / No Myth / etc. labels for it.

      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n

      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I think perhaps the main virtue of a Dogs town and that line about the mirror is that they focus on what's important and minimize wasted effort. I'm a big fan of game rules/instructions that tell you what to focus on. "Make sure you cover these bazillion bases" is not helpful. At the same time, "focus on flexibility!" can be unhelpful too without concrete steps to use in doing so. I wonder if we could concoct something focused and concrete but also suitable for on-the-spot use, like:

      Step one: concoct an obstacle, challenge, or threat (OCT)

      Step two: concoct an apparent way to overcome the OCT

      Step three: concoct a reason, outside of the OCT itself, why the apparent solution is not currently doable

      I'm sure that's not sufficient yet, but I think it's starting to get us some good elements...
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n The core of DitV town creation, in my eyes, goes like this:

      - come up with an NPC and pick what they've done from a list of problematic but understandable actions
      - come up with another NPC who's suffered in some way from the last NPC's action; for them, pick from a slightly worse list of problematic but understandable actions
      - repeat until there's an obvious need for the Dogs' intervention

      If your goal is to create a situation ripe with opportunities for moral judgment, this seems to me like a pretty efficient way to go about it.

      Racking my brains for tactical fantasy adventuring analogs...

      I think there's a big difference in designing for "what can you do" versus "what do you want to do"...
      \n

      \n I'm mostly interested in that step one. "Concoct a toy". Like I placed I think 64 immovable rods in a home-made dungeon -- all synced. When you'd freeze one, they all froze. That was my own idea. They became something the party used a lot over the rest of the campaign. I need to come up with more ideas. Maybe it's practice-makes-perfect.
      Most of my ideas now for the glitchworld dungeon are flavorful but with a low "toy"-factor or "puzzle"-factor.

      One I've used that came from online that I like is "A lake with a strong magnet in the bottom, attracts all metals". I placed that in a dungeon. I had no idea on how to resolve it. I just thought it looked interesting. It turned out to be great in play.

      \n I'm still in love with the first idea that popped on my mind when I read the 5e monster manual and looked at the Animated Object entry. I think it's not hard to come up with something like that when reading it: Place a luxury lobby as the entrance of your dungeon complete with a carpet, old armors and swords on the walls or on the hands of each armor. Animate them when the players are close enough. It's the perfect combo: the carpet immobilizes and suffocates one PC, whoever hits it then deals half the damage to the PC and half to the carpet, animated swords and armor keep everyone busy or running, inmune to almost all effects, yet going out of the area is enough to get out of trouble actually.

      On another use of the same trap, have the players find and armory full of dwarven plates. On the wall, put a sign in dwarven that reads "Dwarves don't forgive those who take which isn't theirs". Animate the whole armory when someone who isn't a dwarf takes anything away.

      Also, I'd reccomend you One page Dungeon you can either use the dungeons there as they are or recycle some parts to make your own, there are a lot of interesting ideas there!
      \n

      That is awesome. Unfamiliar situational assets are the best. Not sure what the keys or steps would be to inventing them. Hmm...

      \n\nWarriorMonk \n\n\n

      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n A couple of the keys and steps that work to me are these:

      -Decontextualize: Take anything that doesn't belong to your particular game setting and translate it into the game. On my last D&D session I included the Smurfs, by using a tribe of goblins painted in blue stripes ala Braveheart. Players didn't noticed until the goblins all went back into the forest singing... but you don't have to make the reference so obvious as me. Take the subway service and adapt it into your setting. You can decontextualize even smaller things to get something completely original. Like the spirit of German Patterson, a sailor that possessed a broken compass and now wants to guide you to his treasure, but keeps giving you strange directions though the way (just came with this on the spot thinking of a GPS)

      -Get to know the base material as much as you can, then pick up two random things and try to connect them. Getting to know them helps you build connections easier, but just trying to find a logical way to make that connection is a great mental excercise.
      \n

      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I'm loving the discussion about useful and focused game prep. (I also echo Dave about dogs towns being a good example of lean and effective prep which combines some solid background with lots of room to improvise and adapt - that, to me, is a golden standard to follow. I posted earlier on how creative agenda is the key to figuring out which technique goes where; hopefully I don't need to repeat that.)

      I want to mention a different angle on "why is no myth so awesome?".

      You see, it's quite different for the player and the GM. For example, Dave is interested in creating a sense of reality for the players, but he doesn't appear to value that consistency himself. He's happy to make stuff up; he just won't show the players that.

      His priority is on crafting an awesome experience for the players. He doesn't need to believe himself that the place he's describing is real: he accepts that it is a fiction which he uses in the service of the game.

      I've played in games that are the opposite, though: the GM really wants to *believe* herself in the sense that the place is real. that GM might be really concerned with keeping notes and double checking facts in source materials, maybe even taking notes as she plays. Fidelity to this real world (e.g. Tolkien's middle earth) is the priority.

      Getting a detail wrong is a failure in this person's eyes; it is a "real" place and it must be accurately portrayed.

      Meanwhile, the players can be enlisted in the process of portraying that world, as consultants or creative collaborators. "What kind of armour would a Qwghlmian guard wear? John, do you remember this? Lucy, based on what you remember about their culture, what would you say?"

      It's the complete opposite.

      So there are all kinds of possible permutations. (At least 4 from the axes I discuss in this post, alone.)

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Right. So why is it awesome?

      I think it has a lot to do with willing suspension of disbelief, trust, and mental discipline.

      As a GM, playing with a sense of full
      Myth (e.g. What's in the next room? I don't need to decide, it's on page 17...) allows you to relax contradictory desires or priorities. Relax mental discipline. You're just presenting someone else's material: you can relax and worry about presenting it well and being fair in adjudication.

      If you're someone who worries about unintentionally railroading your players, being unfair, being perceived as being unfair, and similar things, playing from a Full Myth may be such a relief as to be borderline magical.

      You can play with an easy confidence and a great feeling of freedom. (Some players are like this, too: they have more fun when someone else has told them what they're supposed to do - tell them that they can do anything they want, and suddenly the game is very stressful for them).

      So, these techniques allow you to relax your mental discipline, anxiety, and so on. You don't have to worry so much, because you're just a referee and narrator.

      As a player, a game based on a Full Myth allows you to stop worrying about how and why the GM is doing things. If you have a history of bad GMs, perhaps, you always worry about the GM making things unfair or giving you easy victories.

      Knowing that the GM is playing Full
      Myth lets you relax any trust issues you have and just enjoy the game. It's easier to suspend disbelief if you think that the material isn't coming from your
      GM, or your GM's whims, but an authoritative source. That makes "constructive denial" easier, and allows you to suspend your disbelief with less mental dissonance.

      There is some trust lacking. The player finds their suspicions of the GM's abilities or motives is distracting them from playing the game. The GM effectively says, "hey, don't worry. It's all coming from this source over here."

      Now they sigh a sigh of collective relief and they can relax and play. The players find it easier to trust the GM and immerse in the game, and the GM relaxes because she is no longer worried about her own control tendencies interfering with the game, no longer worried about being judged by the players, and finds the mental discipline required to keep the game process "hygienic" to be much easier to manage.

      So that's my take on it from the social dynamic angle. (The Big Model teaches you to look at the social layer as the outside of the onion, after all...)

      \n As a counterpoint I don't think Full Myth provides even the slightest hint of protection from railroading. If anything, I'd rather that more railroading is done "Full Myth" style, because some GM thinks the world is the important part instead of the players.

      Indeed, almost all of the GM benefits Paul is citing for Full Myth only apply if you're running someone else's content. You can't blame the designer of the module if it's stuff you yourself created.

      And I really, really wish I understood the mentality that "Since the GM wrote this down three days ago, it somehow protects us from his capricious whims.". If anything, it encourages the GM to railroad you into the content he prepped. So this isn't just a sense of feeling safe when you're not, but a sense of feeling safe when you're actually in greater danger.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nIsaac \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      And I really, really wish I understood the mentality that "Since the GM wrote this down three days ago, it somehow protects us from his capricious whims.". If anything, it encourages the GM to railroad you into the content he prepped. So this isn't just a sense of feeling safe when you're not, but a sense of feeling safe when you're actually in greater danger.
      \nYes, there's probably some temptation. Though if you know the players are going there ahead of time (because of scheduling) you can prep a specific place without worrying about luring players there. And from my perspective, stuff prepped ahead of time still can't take into account the players' reactions, which seems to be the crux of the matter.

      Right now I'm understanding it as being analogous to taking satisfaction that a twist in a TV show was planned all along. If the shocking twist was made up out of nowhere, it's much less satisfying. It's why some writers take pains to reassure their fans that they have a plan. It's why the mystery writer in the classic whodunnit is supposed to show you all their cards and make the revelation hinge on facts that were already established, not brand new ones. (Sorry for using plot examples!) If the writer only decides who did it when they see the audience's reaction, that breaks the genre expectation.

      You can write content that doesn't rely on a planned sequence or narrative. It requires much more attention to ergodic structures, that is, rather than being a linear narrative it has a more complex structure that can be navigated in many ways.

      (The above is based on my personal take on it, from what I value about this end of the spectrum.)

      \n As has been pointed out though, there's a whole lot of happy medium going on. That was sortof my argument earlier - that no one really plays completely No Myth for longer than a one shot, because it's virtually impossible to stop yourself from thinking, "Well, they're off to the Crypt of the Sorcerer King next. I bet there's...." but you can easily do a sketchy high level myth and then minimum-myth all the actual stuff that happens in play.

      In fact, that's pretty much what Dungeon World tells you to do.

      But again, this thread was, perhaps poorly, titled "Why is Full Myth so awesome?" not "What are some reasons you might want to use some Myth." And I am still forced to maintain that true, honest to goodness, Full Myth... is just that. A total myth.

      \n Just came across a passage that seemed synchronistically pertinent...\n
      "In the SSC (Shamanic State of Consciousness), the shaman typically experiences an ineffable joy in what he sees, an awe of the beautiful and mysterious worlds that open before him. His experiences are like dreams, but waking ones that feel real and in which he can control his actions and direct his adventures. While in the SSC, he is often amazed by the reality of that which is presented. He gains access to a whole new, and yet familiarly ancient universe that provides him with profound information about the meaning of his own life and death and his place within the totality of all existence.

      During his great adventures in the SSC, he maintains conscious control over the direction of his travels, but does not know what he will discover. He is a self-reliant explorer of the endless mansions of a magnificent hidden universe."
      - Michael Harner; The Way of the Shaman

      \n Ghostly directions: did you ever play Link's Awakening?

      Relax: That rings very true to me. As I've said, I became pretty good at spouting off stuff from the top of my head after a while of No Myth. So when I started using prep, it was unexpected to see some \u201dpsychic ram" free up. Of course I've already filled it again with my weird initiative house rules

      Railroading: It's been my practical experience that railroading has decreased. I agree that it sounds counter-intuitive. But in my olden no myth days, whatever the players did, I would make up what happened next. In my current style, whatever I prep, the players will do something unexpected with it.
      If I ever find the need to railroad, I'll say "Guys, have your PCs go to the old swamp. That's the only thing left on this map that has anything cool on it".

      \nI've answered this over and over again.
      I see it as a scale and I was very, very close (but not all the way there) to No Myth for a decade. You make me feel very stupid about it but I'm trying to resist that emotion, trying to think of it as "Improvisational skills are always useful".
      When I started playing OSR (as much as I don't like what that community has turned into, when it comes to behavior and values outside of games), I was floored. I called my mom, I was like \u201dThis, this is how you were supposed to play all these years!" I started a new campaign, it had greater retention than anything I've ever ran.
      The specific terms "No Myth" and \u201dFull Myth" are confusing I now realize. And because of that, the thread is poorly titled.

      "I've had success with more prep and want to try even more prep." That's the new title.

      That's still pretty far from FULL full myth. But in the olden days, I wouldn't even use a name list. I'm trying to find my place "on the scale".

      \n
      \n 2097 - I think the thing that has been bothering me about your responses to this thread is that I have been couching them in the sense of you doing/wanting to do "full myth" when it seems like everything you say you want points to a thoroughly middling amount of myth. Maybe I haven't been making that point effectively, but I think you need to be looking less at all of these confusing and absolutist game design terms and more at "How much do I need to prep to run the best game I can."

      So if it seems like I've been railing against you, it's because of the way you've framed this discussion as a choice between "don't even think 5 seconds ahead" and "PREP ALL THE THINGS" and seemed to be resisting my attempts to pull you into what I've always felt was the actual, useful intent of 'No Myth' - in that it doesn't mean "no planning ahead" but simply means that most stuff is going to be invented in real time (while 'leading the target') rather than days before.

      So now I'm going to ask straight out - have you considered running a game in this fashion instead of your "absolutely no thinking ahead and make up everything only the very second that information enters the world" style of striving for some sort of "Absolute Zero Myth" play? It just seems like you're rejecting a playstyle based on negative experiences trying to run it in an insane over the top way. It's like someone suggested that you cut back on saturated fat and you did it by giving up on all food except for rice crackers.

      Considered, say, prepping an outline?

      \n When I was a wee lad, I remember reading accounts from players in Professor M.A.R. Barker's Tekumel games. The player said that they could ask Barker any detail about the world, and he produced an answer immediately, and it never seemed like he was making it up. Most likely, he made up a lot of it on the spot, but from first principles about what made sense in the world he'd invented.

      I think the specific example in the article was about sexual practices involving wafers, or something, so it's not like it was going to be a life-or-death detail in a dark temple full of angry priests. (Though, the character could have been masquerading as a harem girl, so who knows?)

      No matter. The player was marveling at how real this Tekumel world felt, as if Barker didn't have to make up details, but just try to remember what they were. That's Full Myth from the player side, but who knows what the GM was doing.

      \n As far as the railroading issue:

      I think it's totally orthogonal to prep or no prep. You could do it - or not do it - either way.

      But! If the stated desire is to play a certain way (e.g. Total sandbox), I can see how some GMs and players would find comfort in a certain method of prep being used. (Which is what I discussed in my last post.)

      It's a complete illusion, of course. (That is, there are methods to achieve the same goals, and these methods don't guarantee that those goals are reached anyway.) But it can still *feel* good. "Freeing up mental RAM", as Sandra says, is a good way to put it.

      \n The browser ate the mom story

      I'll try again.
      First of all, the other post. Did you mean the one about trust and relaxation? I agree with that post.
      I do fill in the gaps by making things up and that immediately starts nagging me, feeling bad, feeling like I'm back to my old improvising ways.



      Second of all, the mom story. Perhaps it's good that I'm retyping it, it could come out better.
      My mom has only played RPGs once, and she was the GM. We were playing a complicated BRP clone. That's the only game we had.
      I was in the game and the rest of our family were also players.

      I don't remember why the session ended. Time, energy...

      I have lots of memories from making characters, from the beginning of the session and up through the middle. This was 22 years ago, mind.

      The next memory is a couple of weeks later. I'm reading the module and the gamemastering guide (neither of which I had read when making my character). I for the life of me do not get it. Actually, only now am I beginning to get it. She follows the module (a pretty railroaded affair, but hey, we were playing along) and filled in the gaps from her imagination. There weren't many such gaps though because we were on the straight track.

      We didn't play again. (Maybe because I read the module? Don't remember if that was a factor.)
      Me and my sister started playing Hero Quest, the board game, instead. She was the overlord.

      What I wrote in that last paragraph, I could not understand to do myself. I couldn't make that leap.

      Well, you've heard the rest my gaming story many times and I'm always glad to tell it again. I ran games and played in games, telling her only that I did so, like "Oh, those guys? I met them from playing roleplaying games" etc.

      But when the mirror story happened -- my second D&D session and my first of many mindblowing events both as a player and as a DM -- I called her up and told it. I thought it was so cool, so real, I was so proud of how we had handled it.
      She was like "Huh. That's neat."
      I don't think she remember's the game we played back then.
      And I wish, I wish we had started with a better game. And I wish, I wish I had had more trust in her even with the crappy game to just keep on playing together, with the family.

      I was embarrassed to play with my family, with my mom as GM, not because I didn't love them but because I didn't have a group of friends to do it with. Nowadays, I'm proud of it, but not back then.

      \nThe reason we're railing against each other is because you're understanding me perfectly. I've swung the pendulum because of excitement about suddenly finding possible ways to prep after first years of shying away completely from prep, then years of not understanding how to prep.
      I'm interested in this farther end of the pendulum because it's cool and new and shiny to me now.

      I fill in gaps with your method, and inspired by you, I'm doing it more. Tonight was the first night of a new campaign. Both character had ties to a band of bandits, one as a former member (still in good standing) and one as a bounty hunter targenting those bandits.
      I looked up the Raider tag in Red Tide and wrote down the ideas, thinking of them almost like elements in a fiasco game "Place: burnt farmstead. Item: Bribes to the raiders. Person: Bandit chieftain". I tied some but not all of my previously created and placed thieves in the city to this bandit gang. During the first part of play, I nailed some of these items down, writing them down. It's creating a richer, more detailed world.
      The players are aware of this process.\n
      1. Did any of that resonate with you? You didn't really respond to my last post, and I'm curious to hear how you feel about it.
      \nIs that the post that has the sentence: "I think it has a lot to do with willing suspension of disbelief, trust, and mental discipline."? I apologize for not replying to it. If that's the post in queston, it resonates a lot with me. It describes the feeling as both as player and as GM well.
      Unfortunately I can't really rely on that "Hey, don't worry" because I fill in gaps a lot. When running a really good module, such as The Lost Mine of Phandelver, I could, and it was amazingly relaxing. Now that I'm running Princes of the Apocalypse, and when I'm running my own campaign, I can't. I've left more gaps (made bigger "blanks") than LMoP did, and so did Princes. So I have to mix in some made up stuff and some from tables and it's not as satisfying for me or for the players.
      But it's what I have to do in order to get the game going.

      Like, an example from earlier tonight.
      They go to an inn. I roll up a random inn from the 5e DMG.
      I felt iffy about this. I don't know why...
      I wished I had remembered to prepare an inn before hand.
      I had made a rumor table, though, so while the patrons were on-the-spot-created, what they were saying wheren't.

      But all this on-the-spot creation disturbs the relaxation you talked about in your post, Paul.
      On the other hand, having EVERYTHING prepped leads to something that is impossible to read and learn.\n
      2. Did you really call your mother to tell her about your new approach to prep?!?
      \nI'll tell the story about my mom and prep.
      My mom is really shy. We talk a lot, but... we mostly talk about me, about how I'm feeling. I don't know her so well but she knows me very well.
      She's only played RPG once. I was in the game -- my first game actually, and her first as well. (There were other players but also related to us! Family game night!)
      It was a BRP knockoff, using a module.

      I don't remember why the session ended... maybe just because of time, maybe something else.
      She put a lot of work into it, reading the game master guide and the module.

      When I later read that same material I was really confused. I had no idea how she had ran it because the module was so full of gaps, so weird, so much time skipping and I didn't understand it at all.
      I switched to playing the Hero Quest board game with my sister as the overlord (re-skinning the characters to make up our own backstories) and my mom never gamed again outside of like tricktaking card games and Scrabble.
      It's a sad story actually.

      When I became grown up I started running games. When I was the GM, I ran No Myth. Inspired by Steffan O' Sullivan but taking it even further. (I didn't understand that even he prepped -- he talked about winging it in situations, I was winging it completely.) When I was a player, there was a cult of secrecy among GMs, so we couldn't learn from each other. The philosophy was that the GM is a magician and the players should just accept what they hear at face value and the GM should never reveal their many tricks. I ran diceless also.

      I didn't really talk to her about how it was going, or tell stories from the games. I just said "Oh, we finally had another roleplaying night, I had a great time" and "Oh, those guys? I met them from playing roleplaying games." I played rarely because I had a low player retention rate and always had to start new groups, living in a very small city.

      Then I heard a story about someone who did play with dice and rules and I got intrigued. I threw away my somewhat fun home-made no-myth games (I read a lot of GURPS modules but only for inspiration, for setting and mood) and started pursuing trying to learn how to run games. I didn't get it... I had bad experiences with SotC, Diaspora and Fate Core (I kept trying Fate because I love 4dF so much, it's a brilliant dice system).

      When the "mirror story" happened, for the first time since I started gaming, I called up my mom and told her something that had happened in the game. She thought it sounded cool. We talk like six times a year or so. She doesn't really remember running that game almost 25 years ago. I wish, I wish we had started with a better game. I wish, I wish I had had more trust in her, she seemed to understand it better than me.

      The "mirror story" was just the first of so many cool things that has happened since I started playing and running dungeoncrawl games. It was my second D&D game, I had played in The Lost City just weeks before and had a good time.
      But the "mirror story" blew my mind.

      I'm mostly interested in that step one. "Concoct a toy". Like I placed I think 64 immovable rods in a home-made dungeon -- all synced. When you'd freeze one, they all froze.
      You know, I've been looking at this from the angle of "what makes a good experience?" and then reasoning abckward to what would produce that, but maybe a simpler approach is to ask, "what makes a good toy?" Like, in general, not just for dungeons or whatnot.

      Brainstorms:
      - at least one obvious use
      - at least one unique use
      - if static, portable
      - if overpowered, not portable
      - re-configurable -- different positions, orientations, shapes, etc.
      - changing configuration changes behavior
      - accepts inputs: allows you to try stuff out on it

      A frisbee can obviously be thrown and caught, it has more hang time and comes in slower than anything else that can be thrown and caught at significant distance, you can take it virtually anywhere, you can flip it over or turning on its side, flipping and turning it allows you to throw it differently or drink out of it, and it behaves differently depending on how you grip it, spin it, hit it, and throw it.

      Dungeons with toys = win? Toy design = dungeon prep?

      \n To put it simply, full myth and an externally fixed world gives you Fiero (i.e. the joy in overcoming challenges) in a way that having the world tailored to your characters doesn't. And likewise a straight GM decision in your favour can feel like they are going easy on you and one against you can feel unfair. Leaving it to the dice means that the challenge is much more arbitrary both ways and so much more of a real challenge to overcome.

      To put it simply, full myth and an externally fixed world gives you Fiero (i.e. the joy in overcoming challenges) in a way that having the world tailored to your characters doesn't. And likewise a straight GM decision in your favour can feel like they are going easy on you and one against you can feel unfair. Leaving it to the dice means that the challenge is much more arbitrary both ways and so much more of a real challenge to overcome.
      \nThat seems like it's at the core of it.

      I'd just add that the structure the player interacts with can feel different for each method, in ways that affect the player's experience: If it is the in-the-moment GM, the structure you are exploring risks becoming "persuade the GM" or "guess-what-the-GM-is-thinking" rather than "interact with an imaginary world". If it's down to the dice, it becomes a game about luck and manipulating the odds, which is why things like Burning Wheel's Let it Ride help, because it makes it harder to game the dice instead of playing the game. But if it was pre-prepped stuff, or extrapolated from the established Full-Myth, it makes it easier to deal with the actual content, rather than the content's delivery method.

      I've been trying to figure out why Powered-by-the-Apocalypse games work so well for me, even though on the surface everything just comes down to rolling enough 10+ results to succeed. I think it might be because the moves ground the game so firmly in the fiction that the narrative logic dictates the way the game goes more strongly than the dice do. There's still some squishiness if the MC decides to go easy on you, but the principles and agenda help a lot with that. (Apocalypse World is pretty explicit that the MC needs to make the characters hurt while still being their fan...)

      I've been trying to figure out why Powered-by-the-Apocalypse games work so well for me, even though on the surface everything just comes down to rolling enough 10+ results to succeed. I think it might be because the moves ground the game so firmly in the fiction that the narrative logic dictates the way the game goes more strongly than the dice do.       \nI think that a big part of it is just how little control the MC has. That the MC doesn't even touch the dice - and a lot of the ways the MC is going to make things hard on the players are dictated by things like the start-of-session moves.       \n\nAirk \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Like, Airk... you've reacted pretty strongly against the notion of completely no myth. So can you then understand the feeling of wanting to move towards the "more myth" point on the scale?
      \nYes and no. The reason I react strongly against the idea of "completely no myth" is because I feel like it would just result in an incoherent game and also actually make the GM's life harder, because in striving for that "absolute zero" point, you throw out the ability to do all kinds of useful things like thinking ahead. I'm essentially trying to approach this from a pragmatic standpoint - what are the gains and the losses for a given level of "myth". Or to put it another way, I don't think either full myth, no myth, or anything in between myth have ANY intrinsic value. The only value they have is in what they add or take away. So the sort of "absolute zero myth" setup where the GM is "not allowed" to think ahead seems to take away a sense of a coherent world (because the GM is constantly working in, it seems, a vacuum) and adds extra workload for the GM because they are not "allowed" to use any of a large number of tricks to help themselves along. Since that is taking away something good (Coherence), and adding something bad (Work!), I don't see value in it.

      However, I think that adding a relatively small amount of myth is all that is needed to avoid those difficulties, and beyond that point, no one has really been able to explain to what the added value is for introducing additional myth into the game - except in the obvious case where writing up tons of world info and the like is simply EASIER for the GM than making it up on the fly. Hence my earlier comment about "Whatever is easier for you to run." I haven't seen anyone in this thread with any solid examples of what actually changes at the table when you introduce more myth - your mirror example, for instance, is a classic case of it being exactly the same with minimal myth. The PCs enter a room with a magical mirror in it. The GM, in all but the most absolute-zero myth types of games, spend a moment and a half thinking about what that magic mirror DOES at the same time the party introduces it. He should not be waiting until it is interacted with. The mirror is there. It has been introduced. The players can do whatever they want with it and it will still do whatever it does. Yes, someone could argue "but the GM could change his mind between then and when the mirror actually does something" and I agree - but the GM could just as easily change his mind about the mirror if it was written down on a piece of paper in front of him. That's the other side of this - there's a lot of mysticism surrounding prep, and fundamentally, prep is not sacred. It is not immutable. It is no more real than stuff the GM made up two minutes before it appeared. If I write down "the mirror petrifies the first person to look into it, then cracks and becomes nonmagical" in my notes, that has exactly as much binding effect on me as thinking it when the PCs enter the room. It's not a shield. I can't say "Well, I wrote it down, so I'm sorry I killed your character, but it's not my fault!" I don't get to sleep better at night because I wrote it down. Nothing changes.

      At this point though, I think it's also important to note that "No Myth" doesn't mean "stuff changes willy-nilly all the time" - to cite the earlier example about the tavern with the low ceiling, and how "now we know that when we return there"; Once established, things stay the same unless there's a reason for them to have changed. If orcs raided that village last month and burned down the tavern, maybe it was rebuilt with a higher ceiling - great - but otherwise, it stays the way it was, full myth or no myth. The world has to remain consistent, independent of how much "myth" you put into your game. Once something has been established in play, it has been ESTABLISHED. The "Myth" is that stuff is established BEFORE it's encountered in play. Once it's been encountered, it stays. I assume that was a ground-level assumption of this discussion, but at this point, I'm leery of any assumptions.

      Excellent discussion.

      I wanted to take a stab at answering the question of what's awesome about being closer to the 'full-myth' end of the spectrum, and one thing that came to mind was videogame sandbox gaming a la GTA. Those games tend to evoke a lot of talk about emergent gameplay, and maybe that's one of the awesome things that 'full-myth' gaming brings to the table but on a greater scale...
      \nHow so? I mean, yes, there are lots of pieces on the table and you can mix them around and stuff. But the price is that you have to invent the 90% or whatever of the pieces that don't get used. If instead you invent the 10% of the pieces that your players see and interact with, you've produced the same feeling of emergent gameplay with 10% of the work?

      I think this is one of the fundamental disconnects I have - people keep floating these statements that say, essentially "Full myth produces result X, and X is really cool." and then I look at it and think "But I could produce result X with minimal myth, and while X is still cool, it doesn't seem particularly correlated to the amount of myth involved."

      \n For some people, there is something inherently fun about having pre-existing elements interact in ways that the creator did not anticipate. If these things are being improvised on the spot because their interaction would be cool, you get rid of the emergent quality.

      That's why the mirror anecdote resonates with me, if I understood it correctly: what made the interaction with the toad sound cool to me is that perhaps the GM had not anticipated that the mirror would be used offensively by the PCs. Even if she had anticipating it, it didn't sound like she was just adjusting the mirror's function during the toad encounter to make everyone go 'cool!'.

      To use a non-gaming analogy, think about movies so bad that they're good comedy. They're for the most part not as funny or fascinating when people set out to make a bad movie on purpose. It's not impossible, but there are relatively few of those.

      \n One area in which Myth is particularly important is in the crafting of mysteries with unobvious but logical and satisfying solutions. Mysteries don't have to be whodunnits - the word can be applied to many features of the gameworld, from ley lines to political conspiracies to magic itself.

      It's not impossible to create a satisfying mystery without any prep, but it is much harder. Like, orders of magnitude harder.

      One area in which Myth is particularly important is in the crafting of mysteries with unobvious but logical and satisfying solutions. Mysteries don't have to be whodunnits - the word can be applied to many features of the gameworld, from ley lines to political conspiracies to magic itself.

      It's not impossible to create a satisfying mystery without any prep, but it is much harder. Like, orders of magnitude harder.

      \nDEFINITELY! Good point! Thank you! So adding Mysteries is a good reason to add some prep.

      Though I do feel that these sorts of Mysteries are pretty much the opposite of all the "Emergent stuff" people seem to be touting as strength of prep.
