      \n I thought I covered this earlier: it's a question of mental discipline and trust.

      Of course there's no real difference between writing down a mirror trap, and improvising one on the spot. The only difference is the GM's experience in creating the trap: what did it FEEL like for her to come up with this mirror?

      Doing it ahead of time is fairly "hygienic": she is, of course, at some level, putting it there in order to make the players' lives difficult, but it's almost as close as you can get to just stating objective facts about some fictional location.

      If the GM is inventing the mirror trap just before the characters interact with it, she may feel some pressure to adapt it somehow to what is currently happening ("Oh, it looks like they're going to want to steal some treasure, and I'm hoping for a tense scene... should I make it a MORE DANGEROUS trap?"). It's possible to come up with methods not to do this kind of thing (e.g. rolling dice, principled GMing, and so on), but it's arguably a little more difficult to be detached than it is when you're prepping before the game.

      As you can imagine, if you move the moment of creation PAST the moment of interaction - "Ok, I grab the mirror and look into it. What happens?" "Uh, well, let's see..." - this challenge becomes even greater. It's even more difficult now for the GM to remain "objective". She may have certain desires for the game to go a certain way, a desire not to hurt that player's character, or whatever else clouding her mental process.

      Vincent Baker said it well a while back (many years ago): anything that's prepared for a game is an illusion. It's just a *plan* you have of introducing something-or-other. It doesn't become real until it is actually brought into the shared imaginary space in actual play. Until then it's just something you're maybe thinking of introducing to the game, but, as far as the game and its players are concerned, it doesn't exist.

      Looking down at the paper allows you to hold yourself accountable, nothing more.

      If it's your aim to hold yourself accountable, it helps. But, ultimately, there is no difference (to an outside observer) between playing a module written 50 years ago and inventing stuff on the fly.

      \n Even the "just enough myth" is way more myth than I used to use. So for me, saying "Oh, I love full myth, I love more myth", that's me going in that direction.
      You're like "Oh, more myth, what's the use, I have enough myth!" while I'm like "More myth? Yes please! I know what it's like to be starved of myth!"
      I don't have binders upon binders with every thing that ever happened in Selte'azda.
      I have a couple of index cards worth of encounter tables and a couple of pages of dungeons, that's what I have so far. I want a little more but it's a tightrope because I don't want to be overwhelmed like I am with Princes of the Apocalypse, where I'm drowning in pages after pages of boring text.

      For some unknown reason, having the mirror be in the dungeon before we even enter the dungeon feels cooler to me. But having it be fully prepared as we enter the room, before we start interacting with it, is as you say, much better than it being decided after the GM knows what we're doing with it. To you, that would be unthinkable -- but to me, I've been there, I've been there so often and to go into something tangible, pre-written...

      And I'm OK with prep happening 30 seconds before the game starts. That's been some of my most awesome prep.

      \n Hi, Sandra! We once played Psychodrame in Dante's basement a few years ago!

      So it seems to me this thread is about Scrap Heap Challenge. It's a show where the contestants get a mission and they have to build a machine from the stuff in the scrap heap. So there are three ways we could do this.

      1: The scrap heap is just there, unrelated to the mission, and the mission is decided and they have to make a plan and find stuff that can be used and improvise and be creative.

      2: The scrap heap has been prepped a bit by the "GM" (the production team) to include things that can be useful for the mission in question.

      3: The "GM" looks at the plan the team is making and then places some relevant objects around the scrap heap where the team will find them.

      1 is total creativity for the team. 2 is more of a "can they find the right pieces?" thing. 3 is illusionism. If the world has been prepped ahead of time, that's like number 1, and whatever the players do with the world is true creativity.

      This actually makes me feel like playing a full myth game, and I'm pretty strict about my no myth preferences. But for me to enjoy it, it'd have to be number 1 above. There should be no scenario, no story, just challenges and a playground and I can take pieces that nobody else imagined would go together and make something awesome with them. I got this feeling long ago when playing the Swedish game "Mutant". It\xa8's a postapocalyptic game and a lot of the point is about roaming the forbidden zones and finding weird stuff from the past, like road signs and plastic spoons. We had a lizard-driven wagon that we over the course of the game reworked into a tank. We armored it with road signs and pieces of monsters we'd slayed, we built a catapult with surgical tubing and kitchenware. We'd build a distilling machine to make booze, which would be used to soak rags which were ignited with a toaster and launched with the catapult.

      All of these things came from a D1000 list of random finds in the zone, and the GM never thought "I'll give them a toaster to light the rags with". We just found random stuff and were truly creative in what we could make with them. And it was awesome. It would have been significantly less awesome if the GM had decided what to give us. The fact that the stuff was arbitrary was a feature, not a bug, of using dice. We didn't want things that would be appropriate. We wanted things that were out of place and pointless, and we'd have to INVENT a point. And that was creativity and that was fun.

      This seems to me to parallel the story about the mirror somewhat. The way the mirror was used was an invention by the players, not a purposeful design by the GM (or the dungeon designer).

      And if that's the case, it can be done with random tables and little myth. And now I want to revisit that Mutant game.

      \n
      no real difference between writing down a mirror trap, and improvising one on the spot. The only difference is the GM's experience in creating the trap: what did it FEEL like for her to come up with this mirror?
      \nI think that how the players experience the GMs act of creation matters to some players. If they know you're winging most of it, it will matter to some because there isn't a persistent world in the notes or GMs mind where their in game actions have repercussions in that world, in some causal way. "The players did X over here and it actually affects Y pre existing element there." I contrast that with players having an effect on the GMs act of creation: "the players just did X, so adding Y would be interesting."

      I think it's questionable whether a GM can detach themselves enough when creating, so in that sense it's an issue of trust. I don't trust the human mind to be capable of ignoring all that context. It just isn't possible, IMO.


      "Vincent Baker said it well a while back (many years ago): anything that's prepared for a game is an illusion."

      I would argue that everything including the SIS is an illusion. To me, a more useful distinction would be to say that changes to prep do not lessen some players' fun so long as they don't know it has been changed. Once they know, that type of player will find that their enjoyment is affected in the same way that retconning things in the SIS would affect others enjoyment.

      Once the game starts, some players enjoyment depends on the expectation that that prepped elements will only change as a result of in-world actions that follow a line of causal logic. The PCs did X, so like a butterfly effect, the repercussions might spread to other prepped elements. This is lost when all the PCs can do is affect the GM's act of on-the-spot creation.

      I hope I'm making at least a little bit of sense.

      It's just a matter of degree. Somewhere it will cross the line for you. But the line isn't a "thing".

      Causal logic, of course, is important. But, again, that's orthogonal to prep. You can use prep to ignore causal logic just as easily as the other way around. (Like when a GM tries to "keep the game on rails" by inventing ever more unlikely explanations for things.)

      \n Consider when you read a novel:

      Does it matter whether the author came up with the twist in chapter 27 (about what that mirror actually does!) while she was writing chapter 26, or before she started writing the book?

      Probably not. What matters is that it's believable, that it fits the existing facts, that it follows diegetic logic, that it fits causally with everything else that is happening.

      Is this harder to do on the spot? Yeah, sometimes. (Though not always.) But to the "audience" the effect is no different.

      \n I think it comes down to, "It's the principle of the thing." Even if the GM's choice of the mirror in the moment would have been exactly the same as if she'd prepped it, it's the principle of hygienic play that matters to some people. That's the "fairness."

      It's like me saying that I don't want to play in a railroady game, even if the GM is so clever that I don't notice the railroading. Perhaps /especially/ if the GM is so clever that I don't notice the railroading. I want the chips to fall where they may, and let me make decisions based on what happens. It's the principle of it.

      \n I have underplayed the whole "tell the difference" because that's not what we're about in our tables.

      We tell each other how we are running the game, what parts are improvised, what comes from random tables and what comes from the module.

      That goes for both of my groups.

      Can I express a desire to play against my friend in chess (without them secretly looking at stockfish on their phone while pretending to make the moves themselves), without having the "could you really tell the difference"? argument trotted out?

      It's not about what it appears to be. It's about what it is

      Let's say you geniunely believed that organic food, or locally grown food, or sustainably grown food, or food with some other type of ecological awareness, was a good thing.
      It doesn't matter that much if it's better or worse on the tongue in that case. It matters for other reasons.

      In the mirror case both would've sounded the same in the ear but what we did, even the fact that we did something, that is way different. Way different.

      As a player I can tell because I DO ASK THE DM
      and my players can tell because I DO TELL THEM.

      It's not some super weird platonic argument "does a tree fall in the forest and you have no ears can you hear it anyway from the mere vibration"
      It's simply:
      "What is the activity we agree to do now?"

      Like, I don't sit down and promise my friend a game of chess while secretly planning to play bridge.
      I wanted to bring in the DramaSystem tokens and got pushback on that -- because that's something we talk about, workshop, experiment with together. I got pushback on the initative system house rules (from another thread) but I stuck with it, listened to the complaints and adressed some of them.

      As Simon reminded me, "story time" games can be amazing -- he referred to a game we played (ostensibly Psychodrame, but we didn't use Psychodrame's rules, we made up our own) where we all made up the story together. No illusion. That's how we do it.

      When we play D&D, of course my friends are gonna be curious "Oh, wow, that was a tough encounter, where did that come from?"
      "Oh, cool mirror, was that in the module already?"

      We have a dialogue -- and this is very different from how my old groups in the mid 00s did it -- because we've become aware that "roleplaying game" means a thousand different things.

      I think that's just it: players who've never had "bad" experiences with this kind of thing - e.g. a railroading GM, or one who tosses causality out the window, or whatever ruins that particular jam - don't really care. It's a learned instinct.
      \nI don\u2019t think that caring necessarily has roots in a negative experience. I think it\u2019s a matter of aesthetics. I can think that having my actions affect the GM\u2019s act of creation is cool in a different way from having my actions as a PC affect a prepped element in a logical causal chain that I won\u2019t see until I get to that affected element. I can appreciate the quick thinking inspiration that goes into the former, but I can also wonder at how pre existing elements interact in a logical fashion that was also unforeseen.

      \n
      Indeed. Note that this is just as true for prep done in advance, however. (Especially if you have an existing group or players and characters and a Situation, which you usually do.)

      It's just a matter of degree. Somewhere it will cross the line for you. But the line isn't a "thing".
      \nI think that there is an existing line between the state of \u201cgame session has not started\u201d and \u201cgame session is started\u201d. In the former, you lack the context that exists when you\u2019re creating in-game, so you are in fact completely detached from the in-game context that exists when the game starts. You can try to anticipate it, but you can only guess what it will be. When you improvise on-the-spot, the game state is always available for you. If the players are privy to what you\u2019re doing, then aesthetic appreciation comes into the mix, IMO. \n
      Causal logic, of course, is important. But, again, that's orthogonal to prep. You can use prep to ignore causal logic just as easily as the other way around. (Like when a GM tries to "keep the game on rails" by inventing ever more unlikely explanations for things.)
      \nSure, but what I\u2019m trying to draw attention to is how causal logic is used in each approach. Improvising on the spot implies using causal logic on the act of creation: X happened, therefore Y should exist because Z. On the other hand, applying causal logic to pre-existing elements while you\u2019re in-game implies sussing out how actions on one element affect another: X happened, therefore Y is affected in this and that way because Z.
      \n
      Consider when you read a novel:
      Does it matter whether the author came up with the twist in chapter 27 (about what that mirror actually does!) while she was writing chapter 26, or before she started writing the book?
      \nI agree with 2097 on this analogy. A book is not interactive\u2026I\u2019m searching inside for why that matters to me, and these things come to mind:

      -\tWhen the book is published it already has a causal logic chain planned out that you can\u2019t break anyway. Going in, I know that it\u2019s meant to be consumed that way. I can appreciate the cleverness of constructing the twist for me to consume it in order.
      -\tWhen playing in a game, there may be a causal logic chain planned, which I can appreciate in the same way I would appreciate a clever series of events in a book. However, when I, as a player, am able to discover another causal logic chain that affects the existing elements in an unexpected way, it is a different appreciation. It\u2019s like, \u201ccool, we discovered something.\u201d
      -\tWhen I affect the GM\u2019s creativity, it\u2019s like \u201ccool, how she came up with that on the spot, in response to us doing X.\u201d If I don\u2019t know she\u2019s making it up, then I might feel like, \u201ccool, we discovered something\u201d\n
      No analogy is perfect but this one was unusually poor.
      A game, especially with challenge elements, isn't a book.
      And it's as if they came up with the mirror while watching me, the reader, react to chapter 25.
      I want to inhabit a world not listening to a bed time story. Thanks
      \nBut can you tell the difference? I'm really still unconvinced.

      To me, this whole thing feels very artificial. It's like "I can tell this food is organic by the taste!" when, honestly, I'm pretty sure you can't.

      I feel like we should run some sort of study. Except that we kindof already have, and we already know that players generally can't tell the difference unless the GM tells them.
      \nI think that the point is less whether you can tell the difference, and more about what it feels like once you know there is a difference. In the former, you\u2019re operating under certain assumptions, so when the curtain is pulled back, it can be a bit deflating to some people. It\u2019s like seeing the technique behind a magic trick: once you see it, it\u2019s not the same. What reaction you have to that knowledge will vary from person to person. Some people could feel somewhat disappointed at the loss of wonder (\u201coh, that was it?\u201d) and others could feel like, \u201cwow! that is pretty clever!\u201d, and I imagine all kinds of reactions and emotions not covered by those two.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nAirk \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I'm fine with the idea that people want to play the game in some specific way. And that if you say "We are playing this game this way" that you bloody well play it that way. I think that's something we can all agree on.

      Where I start to get hung up is on the WHY you want to play it that way. You can't say "It's on principle" here, because the principle is already out of the way - the principle is "You play it how you say you're going to play it." What I am wondering is why people would have a stance on this if it has no impact on their game experience - and quite possibly a negative impact on the GM's experience, since now they need to spend a bunch of otherwise free time on writing stuff down for their game.

      There isn't some kind of objective good in prep. There just isn't. And while people like to say that it's a question of not trusting the GM to be "fair" in making stuff up along the way, I think Paul has done a pretty good job of indicating that really, prep has at best a marginal impact on fairness - because planning to be fair is not the same as actually BEING fair. Maybe it makes fairness more likely, but I don't really think the impact is as great as it is being represented.

      In the mirror case both would've sounded the same in the ear but what we did, even the fact that we did something, that is way different. Way different.

      I'm sorry, I just can't see this. The fact that you did something is all on you. As is what you did. The GM and their prep or lack of it doesn't even enter the equation in this kind of situation. This isn't even a "GM fairness" situation. This is "The GM put a thing there, and we did something cool and clever with it." Which is equally plausible in all kinds of games. Completely orthogonal to myth. The GM made up that mirror. You did something the GM didn't expect. Good on you. The timing of when the GM made up the mirror has no impact on your actions, because, let's face it - even if prepped in advance, there is no way the GM was not thinking, at some level "This will be a cool thing for the PCs to interact with." OR "This is a thing that is appropriate to be here." And the likelihood of him thinking one or the other of those doesn't change with prep. There's no "fairness" to worry about here. This isn't even a "We can't distinguish this via play" question, this is a "we can't distinguish this at all, ever."
      \n
      \n

      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n

      \n\nJuly 2015 \n

      \n
      \n
      \n Anyone who says players can't spot GM improv is playing with players who don't care whether the GM improvises or not.

      Players who do care? Unless the GM uses certain techniques of delivery, those players can tell.

      I am not guessing, I am referring to decades of experience across hundreds of sessions.

      Players care for all sorts of reasons, some of which suck. But let's not confuse "that's a dumb thing to care about" or "my players don't care" with "no one can tell".

      Paul and Airk, there's no reason for you to care about this if you don't want to. Improvise away, and just avoid GMing for groups who are picky about their style of immersion (or have been scarred or whatever). But if you think it's impossible for anyone else to ever know, you're fooling yourselves. Humans are evolved lie detectors; this shouldn't be a shock. Things like dragging syllables or searching glances register with us. Paul, you tend to look down and repeat the beginnings of your sentences when you're improvising. Airk, I've never met you, but unless you're a public speaker for a living, I'm sure you have some giveaways too.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Where I start to get hung up is on the WHY you want to play it that way.
      Here are the reasons I've seen:
      - prior scarring ("Avoid reminding me of bad experiences!")
      - inflexibility ("I've chosen or gotten used to doing things a certain way -- don't do something different!")
      - compulsive nit-picking ("I spotted you making that up. Why do I mention it? Um...")
      - social configuration ("Some content pisses me off, and I'd rather be pissed off at what someone did earlier than at what my friend is doing here in the room with me right now.")
      - lack of accountability ("I wish to soak in the fiction without expending any effort to maintain it's realness; that's all on the GM, who'd better not slip up and do anything that reminds me this is all made up.")
      - immersion ("I'm trying to stay immersed in the fiction, but I find it hard when I see the real people in front of me making it up.")
      - desire for sense of gameworld solidity ("I'm trying to think of the gameworld as a real place, but I find it hard when I see it being invented right in front of me.")

      Personally, I completely understand the last two, and am on board with supporting them insofar as GMing accordingly is still fun for me.

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I think there's also this thought that a good GM can make stuff up and it'll be just as "real" to the players as if a good GM (maybe a different one) did a lot of prep. I think that's sometimes true, but more often than not, it isn't the case.

      As a GM who hates prep and finds excuses (and rules!) that let me avoid prep, let me say that I think it staves from GM laziness, or at least GM time constraints. I don't have the time and willpower to do a lot of prep. I'd rather wing it.

      I am also really good at winging it, from decades of experience. Does that mean that my games are as "real" as Full Myth games? Probably not. And I think that players can tell the difference.

      When you ask, "WHY [do] you want to play it that way?" the answer is, because the player has a specific technical agenda that isn't being fulfilled by a No Myth GM. Because a player can tell the difference.

    Why is Full Myth so awesome? I don't get it

    \n
    \xab123456\xbb

    Comments

      \n

      \n\nAirk \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n The problem is that once again, no prep is ever truly comprehensive, and you're going to have those immersion breaking moments regardless. And I'm sure someone is going to step up and say "Well, you'll get fewer of them." but will you? I suspect that a GM who is accustomed to having heavy prep support who is forced to improvise because the players did something he didn't prep for is going to fail the "can I improvise this seamlessly?" test more often than a GM who is accustomed to making stuff up a lot from a relatively sketchy amount of preparation.

      Here's the other thing. We've had a lot of examples here of people feeling betrayed or cheated or whatever when the GM subsequently told them that stuff was improvised. That means that those people were, in fact, unable to tell that it was improvised by examining the actual play experience. I think this undermines the whole "players can tell" argument. Has anyone had an experience where they could clearly tell a GM was improvising stuff? In a game where the premise isn't just "the GM should make up a bunch of stuff"? I mean, obviously, you can tell sometimes when the GM is making stuff in a PbtA game... but the game system itself FORCES the GM to make stuff up all the time. Or maybe a better question is - has anyone had an experience where they could tell that the GM was improvising world stuff (as opposed to, say, outcomes of actions, which are pretty much improvised all the time, because there's no way to prep them.)?

      I guess I feel like the whole "I'm in it for the immersion and it breaks down if I see someone improvising in front of me" stance breaks down in the face of reality and the fact that no matter how much the GM preps, they're going to improvising stuff all the darn time. So it can't really be a question of "Is the GM improvising?" because the answer is always "yes". (See my earlier assertion that "Full Myth" is in fact a myth because it's impossible.) If it's impossible for you to maintain immersion in the face of a GM who makes stuff up, you'd never be able to maintain immersion. So there has to be something else going on here.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nDreamer \n\n\n

      \n
      This is "The GM put a thing there, and we did something cool and clever with it." Which is equally plausible in all kinds of games.
      \nMore like, "The GM put a thing in there, with full knowledge of the state of the game, and we did something cool and clever with it" The fact that, for example, the GM knows whether the PCs have one hitpoint left, automatically impacts the fairness of what they create. Will they throw them an encounter with a red dragon knowing that they're one hit point away each from a total TPK? Will they give them a break and throw a puzzle? Will they or not put an obstacle if they try to leave the dungeon? There's just not getting around the fact that when improvising in-game you have access to critical information affecting your decision. \n
      The timing of when the GM made up the mirror has no impact on your actions
      \n
      Let's say that the group's "dungeonator" had been destroyed. Would the timing of the mirror's creation have impacted the player's actions whether the GM created it prior to the dungeonator's destruction or after? \n
      , because, let's face it - even if prepped in advance, there is no way the GM was not thinking, at some level "This will be a cool thing for the PCs to interact with." OR "This is a thing that is appropriate to be here."
      \nIt's not the intent what we are arguing about, it's the effects of timing given the context. With things that have been prepped, there is potential interplay that already exists between elements built in; like a ball at rest on top of the hill. When there is no prep, that interplay does not exist as a potential. Some people really like knowing that there is something like the former at play, some don't care, but there are differences to be appreciated.

      In the mirror and "dungeonator" example, I can see how it would be extremely cool to look back and realize there were all these potential paths already set up by the situation. "If we had not gone into this room, we would not have had the mirror as a weapon against the toad" as opposed to, "If you hadn't made up the mirror as we entered the room, we would not have had the mirror as a weapon against the toad." The former hinges on the group's decision, the latter only happened because the GM decided to insert that just in time for us to find-- she could just as easily have decided to put it in the next room or in the hallway and we would still have found it because she found it cool.

      I'm not sure how else it can be explained.

      I think there's also this thought that a good GM can make stuff up and it'll be just as "real" to the players as if a good GM (maybe a different one) did a lot of prep. I think that's sometimes true, but more often than not, it isn't the case.

      I am also really good at winging it, from decades of experience. Does that mean that my games are as "real" as Full Myth games? Probably not. And I think that players can tell the difference.

      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Oh, man. I feel like my point is getting lost here.

      My point isn't that prep is bad or that no one can tell when things are improvised.

      The point I'm trying to make is that it's the principle behind the thing that matters, not when it's created.

      For instance, we can be playing along and come across a mirror. I can say to the players, "Hey, I wonder what this one does." Then I roll on a random table to find out, before they interact with it. Now it's "prepped", because I've settled on a function for the mirror before the PCs do anything.

      Or maybe I've developed a whole system for how magic works, which I can use to decide what happens with the mirror. For instance, maybe I've established that in my world, the only way to access someone's soul is by looking into their eyes, and I've also established that demons can hide in mirrors. The PCs are hunting down a demon and they've narrowed down its location to this room. At that point I can roll a die to see if the demon is, indeed, hiding inside the mirror, and of course there will be some kind of interaction when a PC looks into the mirror (for which presumably the game we're playing has some kind of procedure; if not, we'd need more detail). I'm acting based on fictional causality and principled decision-making, and making it transparent to the players when it needs to be, so there's no deception.

      (Dave, I believe this is what you do with your meta-magic system in Delve.)

      On the other hand, I could be prepping two weeks earlier and thinking, "Hey, Dave is in this game and playing a Wizard. I know Dave likes things with runic inscriptions on them. So I'll say this mirror is covered in runic inscriptions. And I'll waggle my eyebrows at him and make it sounds really fascinating when he looks inside. And then! And then finally I can do that flashback where he's face-to-face with his mentor... yeah, that's the ticket!"

      I could also carefully design the layout of my dungeon to make sure that the players go through encounter A first, then encounter B, and then circle back to their origin for encounter C, and I could keep refining that prep until I've covered every possible contingency, and the players, like mice in a labyrinth, go exactly where I want them to.

      (I remember once writing up a con scenario which was essentially a murder mystery. I invented so much layered backstory and fictional detail that it would be absolutely impossible for the players to figure things out on their own, until I sprung the reveal on them at the end. Of course, at the actual table, the whole thing fell totally flat, because the players kept trying to discover the mystery on their own. When I kept "blocking them", I could very well point and point to my prep and say, "I didn't make this up just now!", but that didn't change how pointless the game felt to them.)

      So I maintain it's more about a question of principled play than "improvised vs. prepped" - although the various comments about, for instance, improvisation being easy to spot, affecting gameplay, and so forth, are quite right. I'm just trying to shine some light on a different perspective which seems to be ignored.

      Sandra is enjoying a feeling off "hands off the steering wheel", which allows her to be surprised by what happens in play. Prep is helping her get that feeling and discover a new sense of joy and freedom and relaxation.

      I would argue, however, that someone in this position also needs to have other tools to handle those moments prep does not - and cannot - cover, so that s/he can handle those situations instead of falling back into whatever negative feelings or patterns came along with "No Myth play" whenever hir prep doesn't cover the current position, and completely freaking out (or at least losing the enjoyment of playing).


      (Massively cross-posted, of course.)

      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Where I start to get hung up is on the WHY you want to play it that way.
      \nFine, we can discuss that.
      Hell, I even started this thread for the very purpose of discussing that -- thence the "Why" in the title.

      But in order for us to discuss it properly you need to throw away that bloody argument about "Maybe you can't tell, maybe you can't tell".
      Can we agree that that line of reasoning is well put in the ground?

      WE CAN TELL BECAUSE WE ASK. BECAUSE WE TALK ABOUT OUR PLAY
      \n
      This isn't even a "We can't distinguish this via play" question, this is a "we can't distinguish this at all, ever."
      \nWE CAN DISTINGUISH BECAUSE WE ASK. BECAUSE WE TALK ABOUT OUR PLAY
      \n
      There isn't some kind of objective good in prep.
      \nI have noticed a subjective good in prep and I want to explore that further.
      The kicks.
      The same thing you've argued for with your "OK, to have at least some morsels of myth (preparing 30 s ahead), that's bloody obvious, no one is running preparing right the very second" statements.
      If that were true then obviously there is some good in deciding a little bit in advance.

      That's your own case from earlier when you said Full Myth was dumb because everyone was running SOME myth because No Myth was super dumb.




      Let us say we are playing a card game. A simple one... War. You compare cards, highest wins, go on til the deck runs out, the end.

      In one scenario:
      Players reveal top card of the deck, compare them, the highest take both cards and put them on the bottom of her deck.

      In the second scenario:
      One player reveals the top card, the other just (without looking) says one card. They try to do it with verisimilitude, they don't say queen of hearts a thousand times, they vary it with a little four of spades, three of clubs here and there.

      Now, they can use this to win (say two of clubs when the opponent reveals an ace, say three of clubs when the opponent reveals a two).
      They can do it to lose, or to keep the game even or "fair".
      Or they can just try to make up what seems right because they don't want to spend a bunch of time reaching over and flipping over the card, they just want to hang back and say card names instead.


      All these very valid and fun and interesting forms of play. But NOT the same as actually turning over real cards.

      I have impaired vision. When I have my glasses on, I see ok but I misjudge depths and it's like looking through a big ball of yarn. When I have them off, it's way blurry and I wouldn't be able to tell the difference between the real cards you turn over, or the made up card names you say. The two games would still be different games.

      God, fuck... why do people always want to suck me into illusionism arguments!? I DON'T CARE. It's not relevant to how we play. If someone wants to do illusion with their group, go ahead, I'm not their players' champion. I DON'T CARE.




      We are playing a game, an activity, a fun pastime where the expressed pleasure is to interact with the prep/module/random tables (a la Simon's scrap heap challenge) and see what we can do with that "sand", with those seeds, with those limits.

      It's not about a story, it's not about sight-seeing in the vistas of the GM's mind, it's about picking something up, something that was placed there before, and doing our best with what we find, with what's there.\n
      This is "The GM put a thing there, and we did something cool and clever with it." Which is equally plausible in all kinds of games. Completely orthogonal to myth.
      The GM made up that mirror.
      \nThe module writer put it there.
      Sometimes, the GM and the module writer is the same person. I honestly know two (very good) DMs who feel iffy about that, they prefer to run modules for the express reason that it helps them feel more objective, more like a judge in the battle of party vs module writer.\n
      The timing of when the GM made up the mirror has no impact on your actions
      \nAs we've said: If the GM had made up the mirror's effect after seeing us be careful with it, our actions would've been the same but our action's impact on the world, our footprint on the slimy halls of Barrowmaze, would have been different.
      If he had made it up after us tying it to our wagon, it would've been even differenter.



      "has no impact on your actions"
      Sure, that's true.
      In a three card monte game, trying to follow the queen... my actions is the same in a rigged and non-rigged game. I try my best to keep up with the moving cards and then make the choice where I think the queen is more likely to be placed.
      So whether the game is rigged or not has no impact on my actions.
      But whether the game is rigged or not makes a big difference on the impact my actions have on the game.




      SUPER IMPORTANT CLARIFICATION:
      I'm using a lot of "cheating" metaphors here.
      I am not saying that it is always cheating to run No Myth.
      I am not even talking about social contract breaches.
      The point isn't "the GM promised us to play this way, they're not, so they're cheating, bad person".
      The point is: there are many kinds of games.
      In poker it is OK to bet high even though you have bad cards.
      But it is not OK to peek at your opponents cards.
      In chess it's OK to leave a queen threatened hoping the opponent won't see (if they're like a kid or so).
      In chess it's not OK to leave a king threatened hoping the opponent won't see.

      In some games making up a mirror effect after the players triumphantly pull away the velvet cover when a toad monster is in front of the mirror is a virtuous, creative, beautiful move.

      I am just saying that there exist a playable, interesting, very appealing game that's about exploring the prep.
      A game I love.

      Right now, I'm not even making a point whether or not "it's evil to promise game A while secretly delivering game B."
      I'm just trying to make the point that game A exists, that game A is different from game B, and that game A is beautiful and I love it.

      The second clarification:
      I'm back to comparing "making up the mirror's effect as the curtain is pulled" to "the mirror came from a module" extremes.
      This may seem like a strawman that will infuriate all "Some Myth"-proponents. "What about making up the mirror as the players enter the room? You exclude the middle".

      Fine. I am pro Some Myth. I want myth. Let's have myth. Let's bring in that middle, it is cool. Let's experiment with making up the mirror (and it's full effect) a couple of rooms ahead, or when they enter the room, or when they notice that there's an object there. Let's try out different things. I am brave enough. ABT = always be testing. What I believe is that making up the mirror before even entering the level is the most awesome and feels the best. That belief can be disproven through testing. Science! So "Some Myth" is still a very interesting part of the scale.

      But in Airks most recent posts they have made my strawman argument against No Myth come real, by going into "there is no difference, there is no objective good, you can't tell the difference, the only difference is principle and the principle is destructive to the GM". Such talk can only come from a heart that is truly against even Some Myth.

      \n
      The point I'm trying to make is that it's the principle behind the thing that matters, not when it's created.
      \nI think random tables add a wrinkle to the discussion because they are impartial-- assuming they\u2019ve been prepped (there we go again!). They don\u2019t care about the context of the game.

      On the other hand, the assumption of a mirror existing at all and that it does something, when that happens (before the game or during) absolutely matters in terms of what kind of enjoyment it brings to the table. \n

      I could also carefully design the layout of my dungeon to make sure that the players go through encounter A first, then encounter B, and then circle back to their origin for encounter C, and I could keep refining that prep until I've covered every possible contingency, and the players, like mice in a labyrinth, go exactly where I want them to.

      (I remember once writing up a con scenario which was essentially a murder mystery. I invented so much layered backstory and fictional detail that it would be absolutely impossible for the players to figure things out on their own, until I sprung the reveal on them at the end. Of course, at the actual table, the whole thing fell totally flat, because the players kept trying to discover the mystery on their own. When I kept "blocking them", I could very well point and point to my prep and say, "I didn't make this up just now!", but that didn't change how pointless the game felt to them.)
      \nInteresting examples, but I\u2019m not sure how prepped sessions that are so-so are proof that the moment of creation does not matter. The pre-existance of a coherent world and emergent interactions is itself part of the enjoyment when things go well. When you ad-hoc, that possibility goes out the window for the stuff that you're improvising.
      \n

      I would argue, however, that someone in this position also needs to have other tools to handle those moments prep does not - and cannot - cover, so that s/he can handle those situations instead of falling back into whatever negative feelings or patterns came along with "No Myth play" whenever hir prep doesn't cover the current position, and completely freaking out (or at least losing the enjoyment of playing).
      \nThis I can agree with. It\u2019s a matter of degree, but I don\u2019t think anyone is aiming for a pure ideal of pure-myth/no-myth. It\u2019s just nice for some people to have more peanut butter than jelly, or vice versa.

      \n it's not about trust
      it's not about cheating
      it's not about tricks
      it's not about fairness
      it's not about honesty
      it's not about social contract
      it's not about lying

      it's about the idea that
      taking a sudoku puzzle and trying to fill in the numbers to satisfy the sudoku condition
      is different from
      taking a blank 9\xd79 grid and trying to fill in any numbers to satisfy the sudoku condition

      it's about the idea that
      building a lego moc from what few random pieces you have
      is different from
      making a sculpture from clay you can mold as you wish

      it's about the idea that
      resolving a situation based on the pre-existing conditions
      is different from
      introducing elements that will fit the drama and the story

      \n\nAsIf \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n edited July 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n My current approach to moderate prep goes something like this:
      - NPCs and NPC groups are given drives, goals, interests and a few have some specific plans
      - Maybe draw a relationship map, in addition to an area map
      - Locations are described sketchily
      - Any location bigger than a room has a small random table for details or encounters
      - Truly significant items/information are placed or noted down for later placement
      - Other random tables from the rules or supplements selected for support/breakouts

      Now I have a "fishtank" (h/t to Rickard) with a bunch of free-moving objects (NPCs) in it. I think about the types of actions the NPCs will pursue once gametime begins, but I do not specify them nor place them in any order. I view the entire plotfield as an object-oriented tableau, not a procedural timeline. When the PCs begin moving around, I periodically glance at my NPCs and decide - just one step at a time - what this NPC is doing or planning to do right now. If the PCs wander around a lot, I start rolling random stuff and improvising based on location. Sometimes information is discovered as it was placed (full myth), sometimes it's placed just when the PCs need it by putting it in a place or in the mouth of an NPC who's in a position to know it - this is what you might call "Flexible Myth" (the data itself is pregenerated but its location in the world is not decided until the data becomes pertinent to the fiction).

      Note (as stated in the other thread) that I am willing to ditch my entire plotfield if the Players begin doing something interesting (to them) that lies in a different direction. Random tables allow me to get sandboxy even when the environment isn't a true sandbox. But since I know what my NPCs want, NPCs actions are still going to happen, whether or not the PCs are in a position to see or influence them. If desired, these NPC actions often allow me to create signifiers that bring the PCs back into the main area of the plotfield again (perhaps "offscreen badness" like "Having escaped the Lich's patrols, you get to the home of your mentor and find signs of a struggle; you determine she has been kidnapped by the Lich!", etc). But if the PCs have well and truly gotten away and are involved/interested in something else, I'll just drop the Lich story and go wherever they're going.

      This is a compromise on prep: it has the potential for wasted work, but that wasted work is a quick sketch, not pages and pages of needless details.

      \n\nWarriorMonk \n\n\n

      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n Please consider that for some groups of players, knowing something is being created on the spot AND yet not telling a word about it, is gaming etiquette.

      For example, On at least three occasions on my D&D 5e campaign, there weren't not enough players to face the session I had prepared. So instead of shrinking the dungeon so the PCs could face it, I gave them the choice to try and risk a TPK or play a flashback adventure that their characters had before the campaign. They choose the flashback, so I had to make a lot of stuff on the spot. Of course, I've got a ton of tools to improvise NPCs, dungeons and traps on the spot, I can pull a couple monster cards of the appropriate level and only make the simplest excuses for reuniting the party so things don't fall apart, but I also relied a lot on the players to help me with the excuses and dismiss everything that could break the immersion.

      The result was a solid session; they had fun, traps were an interesting challenge, NPCs could have used a bit more of prep, but it worked and each short adventure inspired me to introduce some changes to the whole campaign's myth. Of course players knew I was making some things on the spot, but I tried and said my lines with confidence enough so at least they doubted if I wasn't using prep I remembered from another campaign with different players.

      If I had told them during the session that I was making up things, despite being the obvious truth, it would had damaged the immersion they were trying to attain and keep with such effort. What I'm saying is that in a game of make-believe, it's both side's responsibility to keep the immersion. The GM telling players that this is all a lie is being rude; not as much as a GM who forces the players to believe his lies and robs them of coices, but at least as much rude as a player who can tell his GM is lying in an effort to keep the game going and yet ruin the session calling her a cheater.

      \n If you run a campaign long enough, or if you run many different types of games, or if you do hybrid gaming, weird circumstances and subtle GM dilemmas will always eventually arise. That's actually part of the thrill of it, for me. Gives me a chance to mindread the system, flesh out my world, and do some "performance art". I think there's a time and a place for every strategy, including sheer gonzo experimentation. I almost never use the allen wrench in my toolbox, but I'm still glad it's there.

      Oh yeah that thing I said about "wasted work" - that's kind of a lie. Because it's almost always reusable. Especially if it's still in sketch form. Eventually that detail or character can be used somewhere.

      \nI'm sorry I'm frustrating you. You are frustrating me also, but I'm trying my best to be level and clear here, and I think these distinctions are important. I am sorry that you don't. I believe the games are the same. If it walks like a duck and quacks like a duck, it doesn't matter if it hatched from an egg or was spawned from a replicator, it's still a duck.

      I think we're also falling prey to terms again. I have been arguing against 'pure no myth' here - by which I mean 'No forward planning, no thinking about anything until the very moment it HAS to come out of your mouth' - in part because I think it seems like needless work for the GM, who has to actively struggle to not do those things, and in part because I think the actual INTENT of "No Myth" is not "no thinking ahead" but simply that things can change as long as they haven't yet been established by play. Which is to say that, if you want, you can have "some" myth there.

      There are advantages to prep. It has not been my intention to ever assert otherwise, but I have been very frustrated with the examples people have been using because I view them as false. We have established one very good benefit in the form of Mysteries. Prep can also be useful if it's how your mind works, if you like thinking about the game outside the game, if you are trying to track situations or actions that are too complicated to conveniently keep in your head, or if you you think writing things down keeps you honest. But I rebel against the idea that somehow the players achievements (Such as using "The Mirror" creatively) are somehow different if the GM wrote something down vs the GM making something up 30 seconds before it appeared.

      That's it. That's all I have. Sorry. I didn't join this thread with intent piss people off and get cursed at.

      I think we're also falling prey to terms again. I have been arguing against 'pure no myth' here - by which I mean 'No forward planning, no thinking about anything until the very moment it HAS to come out of your mouth'
      \nIt seems to me nobody has argued against you here. In fact, has anybody even played in the way you describe? It seems like you're arguing against a theoretical construct you made up yourself. Which is to say, a straw man.\n
      But I rebel against the idea that somehow the players achievements (Such as using "The Mirror" creatively) are somehow different if the GM wrote something down vs the GM making something up 30 seconds before it appeared.
      \nDo you not agree that if the GM knows the game situation when she makes it up, she might make up a different thing than if she doesn't? The GM might make something up that's relevant to the situation, to the personalities of the characters or players, to the overall plot arc. The GM might make something up that fits. She might think "Jenny's character has self esteem issues, so let's make a mirror that shows you looking really ugly" or "The group has been having problems with fire elementals, so I'll have them find an ice sword".

      That's the difference. What Sandra wants is something without intention, without a plan for how it's to be used, to how it fits. Because if there's no intention, anything the players do with it will be their idea, their invention, their creativity, their luck. Not the creativity or planning of the GM.

      Obviously this is not all down to prep. Putting an ice sword in room 4 and fire elementals in room 5 would be the same thing, which I'm guessing Sandra would also find off-putting (correct me if I'm wrong here, Sandra). But planning is definitely a part of it, because of the "mental hygiene" issues discussed above. Whereas if there's an ice sword in one dungeon and fire elementals in another dungeon from another module by another author, then that's emergence, and luck. And that's different.

      \n If you think prepping helps you remain impartial, great; It won't save you from yourself, but it can help, assuming that you can resist the urge to change it, and that you are incapable of resisting the urge to do it when inventing things on the fly. It feels weird to me that you can't trust yourself to make up something fair, but that you can trust yourself to not change something fair, but then, apparently writing stuff down helps people stick to diets and new years resolutions and stuff too, so it must work for some people.

      The problem is that people keep showing up with bad examples. A GM who is prepping a scenario the week before will still know about Jenny's character's self esteem issues, and the problems the party has had battling fire elementals. Okay, MAYBE not the fire elementals, if they're only encountered that one evening, but "let's not give them a weapon that is expressly designed to fight the foes they've been struggling with for the last ten minutes" strikes me as the most trivial form of self control imaginable, so...

      I guess what it all comes down to is that I agree in principle, but have found very few instances where I agree in practice.

      \n Yeah, I tried to say the same thing earlier. The writing-down helps, and coming up with the idea before the players interact with it helps, but ultimately just one or the other can be sufficient, and, ultimately, all that matters is that you have the right combinations of principles and mental discipline.

      This is why I posed the "telephone GM" thought experiment earlier: if someone else was coming up with the material, and you were just presenting and adjudicating it, would that be just as fun, or not? I think your answer reveals quite a bit about what particular kind of help *you* need, and which parts of the equation you care about.

      A GM who is prepping a scenario the week before will still know about Jenny's character's self esteem issues, and the problems the party has had battling fire elementals.
      \nRight, which is why it's been suggested that it's even better to be playing a module designed by someone else. Prepping your own stuff is not as good, and making it up as you go even worse.\n
      Okay, MAYBE not the fire elementals, if they're only encountered that one evening, but "let's not give them a weapon that is expressly designed to fight the foes they've been struggling with for the last ten minutes" strikes me as the most trivial form of self control imaginable, so...
      \nBut that's also taking into account what has happened. It's just as bad to refrain from giving the group an ice sword because they would need one. The point is that if the group does find an ice sword, it's down to luck, not design. It's like the people talking about metagame information. If I know there's a trap but my character doesn't, I can only choose to avoid it or walk into it. I can't un-know it and act as though I didn't know there's a trap. Maybe I would have suspected something? Maybe I would have checked with my eleven-foot pole? It's hard to say, so the player who abhors metagame information would rather not know. It's the same principle of "mental hygiene".

      But that's also taking into account what has happened. It's just as bad to refrain from giving the group an ice sword because they would need one. The point is that if the group does find an ice sword, it's down to luck, not design.
      \nHow about just giving them one when it makes sense and there is a reason it would be there? This, to me, is the only acceptable reason for something to be there. Not "because I rolled it" - why would the ice sword even BE in the fire dungeon? And not "because it's in the module" either. Running a 3rd party module just means you're passing the buck here, which to me, is a pointless measure. It's like saying "I was only following orders!" as a way to absolve yourself from guilt.

      And anyway, running a third party module doesn't mean the sword is "luck, not design" either - it just means that someone ELSE designed it that way. \n

      It's like the people talking about metagame information. If I know there's a trap but my character doesn't, I can only choose to avoid it or walk into it. I can't un-know it and act as though I didn't know there's a trap. Maybe I would have suspected something? Maybe I would have checked with my eleven-foot pole? It's hard to say, so the player who abhors metagame information would rather not know. It's the same principle of "mental hygiene".
      \nSo are you suggesting the GM shouldn't even read the third party module? This is getting kindof contrived and it just makes it seem like people don't trust THEMSELVES. Which is funny, because of all the discussion about trust in this thread.

      Do you really have so little idea what you "would have done" in normal circumstances? If your standard procedure is to check with your 11 foot pole, you should probably do that, but if this isn't something you normally do, you probably shouldn't suddenly say "Y'know, it's been a while since I tapped the ground it front of me with my 11 foot pole..." Are people really this mistrustful of themselves? I have trouble understanding why a person who can't trust themselves to have an honest idea what they might have done would, say, trust themselves to not cheat at dice. They seem so rooted in the same fundamental issues.

      Edit: And NO, I'm not saying that people who need to rely on prep are cheaters or something. I'm actually more curious - do people who rely on lots of prep WORRY about cheating? I seem to recall 2097 having strong feelings on the topic of fudging?

      Maybe it's all just comes down to trusting yourself or not. And if writing stuff down helps you trust yourself, go for it.

      \nYes to this.
      Thanks for reading me charitably after all my outbursts, Airk.
      Yes I do believe it can help.\n
      It feels weird to me that you can't trust yourself to make up something fair, but that you can trust yourself to not change something fair
      \nI have a story from my No Myth days. It's a Low Myth example rather than No Myth, because this is what happened.
      The players came to a well where they witnessed a big crocodile who was putting a lid on the well using its crocodilian paws. We were playing diceless and rules-less.
      Here I low-mythed a bit (instead of No Myth) and decided "If someone confronts the crocodile, they'll be killed. It is stronger than them" in my head. This was your typical "30 seconds ahead" example.

      By the way, this whole example is gonna be pretty low on player agency -- that was a problem that was typical to my no myth / low myth days (but not necessarily a problem of all no myth play, contrast mine and Simon's O Coco game which had lots of agency and surprising twists).

      One PC did go up to the crocodile and try to fight it. I said "OK, the crocodile kills you", in line which what I had decided earlier.
      He said "No way" and looked angry.

      I was like "Oh..ok.... uh... he bites your arm off, then!"

      He said "That's bull shit!" and I backpedalled once more to "The arm doesn't come off completely, it's more like a ring of teeth marks around the arm socket. You're gonna get scars but the arm itself is intact".

      Right then and there I thought deep in my dark heart: "Diceless no more!" but it took me another several years until I also realized that I needed more prep. Just deciding on the spot that the crocodile has a d100 rather than a d12 in whatever stat still has the same problem as just deciding on the spot that the crocodile will kill rather than scar the PC.

      But back to the point of the example:
      Prep is social armor. If you want to portray a living and dangerous world to interact with meaningfully, it's very hard to do that without something written down.
      If players these days would dare to try to pull off something like that player that just wouldn't die to the crocodile, I could point to my prep that said "See page this-or-that in the MM, but there are five of them" and then look in the MM and see "The this-or-that monster really has that stat!".
      \n
      The problem is that people keep showing up with bad examples. A GM who is prepping a scenario the week before will still know about Jenny's character's self esteem issues, and the problems the party has had battling fire elementals. Okay, MAYBE not the fire elementals, if they're only encountered that one evening, but "let's not give them a weapon that is expressly designed to fight the foes they've been struggling with for the last ten minutes" strikes me as the most trivial form of self control imaginable, so...

      I guess what it all comes down to is that I agree in principle, but have found very few instances where I agree in practice.
      \nPrepping week-by-week instead of prepping the whole game world (and tables) upfront is something I do, but it is a significant compromise. It's not my ideal and I can see lots of similar problems like the afore-mentioned "strong-against-fire" weapon.

      Arguing from a position of extreme skill at compartmentalization and psychological strength and objectivity and neutral point of view is something that is very far from the DM's I've played under. I've witnessed plenty of problematic calls in that vein.\n
      Right, which is why it's been suggested that it's even better to be playing a module designed by someone else. Prepping your own stuff is not as good, and making it up as you go even worse.
      [...]But that's also taking into account what has happened. It's just as bad to refrain from giving the group an ice sword because they would need one.       \nGot to say that I agree 100% with this. Thank you Simon.\n
      Running a 3rd party module just means you're passing the buck here, which to me, is a pointless measure.
      \nIt significantly transforms my role from "uneasy mix of adversary+referee" to just "referee".

      This is golden dynamite folks. This thread paid off, after all -- I am getting some insight in why lots of myth is so awesome.

      This is something I'm gonna put in a cross stitched tapestry in the hallowed halls of gaming along with Czege's and Lumpley's principles:

      "Having lots of prep significantly transforms the DM's role from 'uneasy mix of adversary+referee' to just 'referee'."

      This principle is called: "The tangible system principle".

      This "lots of prep" can take the form of fronts, moves, prepared 7-9 choices, agenda, and even the entire managed conversation structure in Apocalypse World.

      It can also take the form of a detailed, jaquayed, trap-filled 3rd party D&D module, or roll-tables, or a hex map.
      \n
      Maybe it's all just comes down to trusting yourself or not. And if writing stuff down helps you trust yourself, go for it.
      \nThere's another component and that's the players trust.
      No, not "trust that you're not cheating, trust that you're not fudging or forcing", but "trust in that their actions have real consequences and their choices have real meaning".
      If they can trust in the "system", the social armor that the rules and the prep provides, if they can trust in the feeling that they are interacting with something tangible, something with consequences that they can't plead with or be manipulated by... awesome gameplay happens. Like Simon's toaster lighter or our mirror.

      I was watching Swan Song earlier this morning, I'm still catching up and I'm only at week 14, which I just finished.
      Without spoilers, I'll say that the crew was in a situation that was very high stakes.
      In that situation, I really missed having a very clearly rules-managed way of resolving those high stakes. Instead, it seemed like Adam was very much winging it with die rolls that didn't seem to matter. There were some jawdroppingly awesome roleplaying and social moments, and within those, real choices were being made, but there were also some die rolls that were just like "Why did you even roll that, if there was no consequence to the roll?"

      \n@Airk, correct me if I'm wrong, but it seems like you're pursuing logical consistency in this thread based on language and theory rather than on the actual play and actual people that other commenters are referencing. Like you, I'm happy to challenge and be skeptical of play styles that seem weird to me, but at the same time, I try not to conflate my own preferences with some sort of objective correctness. If you have no interest in ever playing the "trust a page in a module more than anyone at the table" game, cool, but that's just your preference, even if other commenters haven't yet described why it works for them to your satisfaction. The proof is in actual play -- if it does work, then it is a good technique for them in that situation.

      I say this only in hopes of forestalling the next round of "but that's nonsense"/"no it isn't" frustration.

      Here's something I'd like to respond to, which also happens to be a perfect example of what I'm talking about:\n
      How about just giving them [an ice sword] when it makes sense and there is a reason it would be there? This, to me, is the only acceptable reason for something to be there.
      Right: to you. This is a statement of taste. In the context of varied play priorities, there are a million other acceptable reasons for an ice sword to enter the fiction at any given point, e.g. "it's fun", "it's hilarious", "it opens a mystery", "you earned it", "you just spent the points on it", "it reveals a new side of the setting", "it's part of a specific person's vision which we're exploring", etc.

      Maybe you're already on board with this. But maybe not:\n
      Running a 3rd party module just means you're passing the buck here, which to me, is a pointless measure. It's like saying "I was only following orders!" as a way to absolve yourself from guilt.

      And anyway, running a third party module doesn't mean the sword is "luck, not design" either - it just means that someone ELSE designed it that way.
      Agreed, it is a result of someone else's design and a form of deferring responsibility. These are often functional practices. Just because you don't use them in this way doesn't mean there's guilt that needs to be absolved. (If you want Simon to explain what he gets out of these practices, cool, but I'm kind of struggling to read you that way -- sounds more like just "no, you're wrong" to me. If that's my misread, then I apologize, but I don't think I'm the only one in this thread who's taken your comments that way.)

      My perspective on deferred responsibility is that it can be vital to:
      1) Making the RPG experience more game-like
      2) Relieving social burdens

      It is completely possible to roleplay with no system beyond "player narrates actions, GM decides outcomes". I've done this and enjoyed it greatly. It is a very specific sort of feedback, though, with players free to interpret the GM's statements in various ways, from "I'm discovering the fiction-based principles by which the GM adjudicates" to "my friend likes me." In a free-flowing exploration where we're all just enjoying roleplay for a sense of transportation or creative release, then this tends to be just fine.

      On the other hand, if there's a game-like pursuit of a goal where success is not guaranteed, then pursuing success quickly gets tricky. Suddenly it matters whether we're system-learning or people-reading. And not everybody who'd be on board for one is on board for the other.

      Accordingly, we get an infinite number of techniques for "passing the buck" -- everything from elaborate pre-written campaigns to the simple practice of rolling dice. A huge percentage of RPG system design is exactly that: ways for the play group to defer responsibility. Many gamers seem to find it less creatively taxing, and feel that they're getting more bang for their buck, both in terms of "I paid money for a game" and in terms of "this game is unique and not just what my friends and I would have made up independently".

      I'm not trying to say that every roll-on table in a module is actually a good roll-on table. There's plenty of awful game design and module design out there. I'm just saying that "let the module decide", like "let the dice decide" has its proven advantages for those with certain (common) preferences.

      \n Interesting (and excellent) discussion.

      Here's the best summary I've seen so far (at least in terms of what I've been trying to say:\n

      Maybe it's all just comes down to trusting yourself or not. And if writing stuff down helps you trust yourself, go for it.
      \nThe crocodile example is quite good. It wasn't made on any kind of principled basis, it wasn't communicated to the players, and it didn't present any good choices. The right expectations just weren't there (clearly the players didn't think death was on the table).

      For instance, if the game was Call of Cthulhu, and the characters were hapless, human investigators, that same outcome might have been just right. (Whether it was improvised or not.)

      I've seen just as many module-writers (or GMs preparing their own material) doing the same thing: arbitrarily deciding that a certain challenge can't be overcome. ("I don't want the PCs going through this door, so I'll say there's a huge monster. They're first-level, so 4d6+13 damage, AC -4 should do it.")

      What makes that OK in some games is not the nature of the monster/challenge. It's correct expectations. When you run a module, you say, "OK, I'm running this module, and there may be opponents you can't overcome. I'm not gonna fudge in your favour; it's up to you whether you decide to face mortal risk."

      Right?

      Clearly you did not do that in this game. If those expectations had been there (like the way they can often be assumed in a Call of Cthulhu game, zombie apocalypse, or a similar "instant death" genre), I'm sure you wouldn't have had a problem with the players.

      But the other side is that *you* didn't like how it felt to make that call. It was creatively and socially uncomfortable. (I know that feeling very well!)

      Relying on prep can really ease this aspect. But, of course, only if the right expectations are there and the prep itself is principled in some way.

      (Consider a D&D game where a GM stats up an NPC to be unkillable, completely outside the scope of what's expected: most monsters and adversaries have been 1 HD to 3 HD creatures, and the players are all first level, but the GM decided in advance that her favourite NPC has a ridiculous armour class and 4,000 HPs. The players will be just as likely to call shenanigans unless the fact was somehow communicated in advance, even though it was "prepped".)

      As a player in *any* game, I always want to know what the risk level is - what's at stake - or, failing that, I need to know very explicitly that I do not and will not/cannot know the risk level. Just playing a game which seems pretty safe and then someone suddenly announcing I'm dead is, of course, going to cause some very unhappy reactions. That's not playing fair!

      You also make an *excellent* point about a different sort of trust: "Do I have a real chance of making an impact on the game through my choices?"

      This is another thing where, sure, prep helps to a certain extent, but still isn't the crux of the issue.

      I still have to develop trust in the GM and in the processes of the game over time, prepped or not. If one dungeon has a deadly mirror, and another contains a special artifact which allows you to look through someone else's eyes, I can try to use that to defeat the mirror trap. But the GM still has to make a call! She can't just blindly rely on the prep, not every time. No prep can be that thorough.

      A principled GM will do so in some way which I can understand and trust. A bad GM might go too easy on me (sure, anything you try works and you always succeed!) or decide to make a ruling that this artifact doesn't interact with this mirror: seeing through someone else's eyes still exposes you to the effects of the mirror trap.

      At some point in the game, the GM has to judge player plans. At some point in the game the GM has to decide whether certain player actions have long-term consequences or not.

      Did something we did have an impact on the wider world? Can I make a mark on this situation? Do my actions matter?

      Trust in the GM has to be developed no matter what. A GM has enough powers to screw you over no matter how good the prep is. There is always a believable ruling within reach which could be unfairly in your favour or not. (An excellent example is a Charmed monster who is now an ally of the players. It's actions can't be prepped in advance; you, as GM, have to set some kind of principles for yourself to adjudicate its actions, and then stick to them. Over time, the players see whether you stick to your principles or not, and trust develops - or doesn't.)

      \n I think the advent of explicit conflict resolution and stake setting was an attempt to cover some of that. But we still like our minute tasks in dungeons! Paul, did I ever show you my Cascade system? Basically, task dice influence conflict dice, and for each task performed, we see how that changes the overall situation on various scales. So each time you stab the monster, the GM has to address whether that also got you closer to beating the room and the dungeon. It's a bit game-y and meta-transparent for my RPG tastes, but I think it works well for what it is.

      \n
      \n So the important difference between the full-myth play style we're discussing here, and the no-myth play style exemplified by the GM-less "O Coco" game I played with Sandra a few years ago, is that in the collaborative story-building game, there's no referee role, and no need for one. This play style does not require that players can "lose" and be disappointed. The D&D-type play style, however, requires there to be a real risk of failure for the victories to be meaningful. I think this is important. The prep will then be a way to make sure that the GM can feel like an impartial arbiter and the players can feel this, too. When success or failure occurs, it's due to the actions of the players, to chance, and to the consequences of the imagined physical reality of the game world. It's not due to the social reality around the table, or the dramaturgical rules of how stories are supposed to work. And even if this had been true without the prep, the subjective feeling of impartialness would have been different.

      \n Maybe I'm misunderstanding a bunch of stuff, but from the latest comments (that more and more seem to be polarizing in "yes, that's it, this is the point") somehow I get the picture that more-Myth is better than less-Myth for challenge-centric play, because of reasons.

      Any number of no-Myth games help improvise logical consequences for PC actions, but the key element stressed in the discussion is that they must be FAIR, that the GM has to be a REFEREE.

      Any number of no-Myth games offer lots of agency to PCs, but the key element stressed in the discussion is that they must have intrinsic VALUE, success must come from the RIGHT idea at the right time, because it is objectively a FUNCTIONAL solution to a problem... and likewise failure should come from bad choices.

      Telling a story together seems to be not enough. But then even if the story is mostly a GM fabrication dynamically adapting to PC actions, it seems to be not enough. There has to be a sense of "objectiveness" that validates PC choices as good or bad. It's a puzzle, with maybe lots of possible solutions that are better or worse depending on the situation and the personal stakes involved... but a puzzle, a challenge, nonetheless.

      And the general consensus seems to be that more-Myth helps to perceive such elements as present in the game.
      But maybe that only because, as far as I know, there are very few no-myth games with a focus on solving challenges.

      I'm thinking about all the GM-Less games I know, and they all focus on "drama"... having a good dramatic story emerge from gameplay. And of course there are challenges and failure and death, but they are not the point, just tools to drive the drama home.
      But you see how in such games FAIRNESS would be absolutely useless, and its lack is actually the engine of many events!
      You see how the judgement calls work on completely different assumptions: something might be judged as OK not because it offers the best logically-imagined-performance but because it provides the most compelling plot twist!

      I'm thinking of Polaris, SHOCK, Fiasco, The Quiet Year, Dog Eat Dog, etc...

      If this theory is right, then maybe Sandra (and the others) might find the same things they enjoy in full-myth games also in no-myth games, provided they are of the right kind, offering mechanics and structures that support THOSE values they are looking for.

      Am I completely off track here?
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I think that's exactly right.

      Sandra (2097) is operating from the assumption of a sandbox adventure game which challenges the players.

      To be able to create and adjudicate challenges "fairly" (and without biasing the flow of events too much in any particular direction), it really helps to use some no-Myth techniques. It makes the GM's job much easier.

      \n Telling a story is not enough!!! I think telling a story is pretty low on the list of priorities of what I'm interested in when it comes to D&D.

      \n And it's... kinda less about solving challenges as it is about playing with worlds, roles, personalities, interactions and interesting devices and traps. Drilling a little window into the other world to see what happens, playing to find out.
