      \n Like, it's not like Zelda where there is a way to solve the dungeon. It's more like tossing a bunch of darts at a wall and seeing what makes something amazing "come to life". Like the toaster lighter.

      \n So to answer Hasimir's question: It becomes a question of definitions. Is running from a huge list of random tables considered low or high myth? Because that can be fun.

      \n
      Is running from a huge list of random tables considered low or high myth?
      I think it depends on how tightly those tables are coupled to (or representative of) a particular logic, order, setting, genre, theme or topic, as well as to each other. So it rather quickly becomes a system design-approach question.

    Why is Full Myth so awesome? I don't get it

    \n
    \xab123456\xbb

    Comments

      \n
    • \n
      \n

      \n\nHasimir \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2015 \n edited August 2015
      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n mmm... I'll offer a personal example.

      EDIT: As usual I write too much. So for the sake of everyone's mental health I'll offer a TL:DR version:
      - Sandra I get what you mean
      - I like that feeling too in my games
      - I think it might be interesting for you to check either FateLess or Tactical Ops ... give them a shot, play them, then let me know if that kind of gm-less no-myth play feels like full-myth play to you, or not (more or less, obviously )

      - - -

      The long version >_<

      When I am a Player with my own PC I like to focus on my PC, think about her desires, her motivations, her goals.

      Also, I enjoy a lot exploring the setting... I remember breaking the balls of a GM in an oooold Forgotten Realms campaign because I read something about Netheril and it was all vague and mysterious and full of the promise of power... and I wanted to go explore, find answers, discover what was what.
      But he was not into it, and his story was going somewhere else anyway, so yeah he dropped here and there a hint for my sake, but we both knew that things wasn't going anywhere... and it was very frustrating for me.

      Also, I like to feel clever and exploit stuff, be creative.
      I loved creating (in D&D3.whatever) magical items out of low level spells (and I mean very low, cantrips and lvl1).
      At 3rd level my fellow wizards were able to cast a fireball and spent their money on some cool items that gave numerical bonuses here and there (rings, wands, armor).
      I instead got me some Create Wondrous Items talent and spent a FRACTION on what they spent to create one or two items with a combination of cantrips and lvl1 spells ... they got a fireball? ... so what, I was literally a superhero! (still dead in a physical fight, but I could usually smart my way out of those)

      I am the kind of player that when confronted with a huge knight in shiny armor thinks of throwing water all around and zap him with an abismal low level electric blast of some sort... and expects to get the logical result... and let the standard 1d4 damages go to hell.

      I once wanted to have an Harry Potter rpg just to test out an idea I got:
      in that world Wands are both an enabler and a catalyst for magic; and are somewhat living, with a mind of their own; but are just an item that can be broken, stolen and most of al disarmed... there is a specific spell (expelliarmus) to do just this one thing!

      So... HOW exactly is a wand made? Could it have a shape that is not wand-like? Can it be pulverized and made into ink? Can I tattoo a "wand" on a wizard? Can the wizard's body BE a wand? Etc...

      - - -

      Now, answering by myself to such questions is absolutely NOT fun (for me).
      I need external validation.
      I need an authority figure to waves off the stupid 1d4 damage and say "the shiny knight is at your feet, indeed unharmed, but zapped unconscious".
      I need an external source of lore to tell me all about the mysterious Netheril.
      Etc...

      Yes, I might have my own expectations about such things.
      I might like to contribute with ideas and details to the world, here and there, IF and WHEN I feel inspired.
      But to explore something it has to be unknown to me, external.

      Now, in most gm-less games that I played so far this things don't really feel the same. They are there, you can explore and exploit and have creative ideas... but it feels different... for all the reasons explained in the past 5 pages.
      The games I played all focused on telling/living together a compelling and dramatic story: Fiasco, Shock, Polaris, Montsegur, PsiRun, even Hell For Leather.

      I love those games.
      But the feeling was different.
      This is why I came up with a couple of games that could allow me to
      play with worlds, roles, personalities, interactions and interesting devices and traps ... but at my conditions:
      - gm-less
      - zero prep
      - clever ideas beat number crunching any day of the week (or even better, avoid the conflict between the two things and have clever ideas translate to better numbers, and numbers encourage clever ideas)
      - unexpected turns of events, mysteries, plot twist... that actually make sense... random elements that end up looking and feeling as if someone plotted them with care all along
      - and some other stuff

      I don't know, maybe with THAT kind of structure to support actual play at the table, even a gm-less no-myth game could feel (almost, kind of, pretty near) like a full-myth game?

      [moved the game links on top for clarity]

      \n Who are you asking? (I'm not sure.)

      I can say that, personally, I've experimented with a variety of "no prep" techniques which create the sense of "No Myth" in the players. So I know it's possible.

      "Adventure challenge sandbox" is maybe not the ideal scenario for that kind of thing, however. There are a number of clear advantages to prepped challenges in a Gamist context, as we've discussed here.

      \n\nWarriorMonk \n\n\n

      \n
      \n Hasimir, what Sandra (2097) loves from full-myth are precisely those clever artefacts, traps, clues and stuff that create mystery, horror, drama, epicness, etc. when players interact with them, as we all do. But creating all that means a crushing amount of prep, unless there's a trick for it, one (or a few) which it seems you know of. Care to tell us a bit more about it? I've got a few of my own too, for example:

      -I've designed a deck of cards with several random tables, that allow me to make clever traps on the fly, along with random dungeons. It's system-agnostic since it creates the traps around 2 things: the trigger and what the trap does. Rooms need three things: a shape, what and where is the exit, and a detail for players to find if they search the room (which may or may not be connected to the trap). I've got good results using this while playing but it doesn't mean you can't use this for prep and write down everything you get.

      -The first deck I designed, which I called the ResourceFULL deck. It's meant to throw random words and images at you as inspiration to create places, characters, plot twists and more. Again, it's both a tool that can be used while playing or better, before the session to make the prep more coherent.

      -I'm still working on a complete set of monster cards for 5e. When I'm done, I'd probably shuffle them, draw 5 randomly and try to write a chain of events to relate them to make an adventure. May or may not work, but I've gotta try it anyway.

      -Pathfinder's Gamemastering guide has great advice on making your prep and some really nice random tables to help you there. Also, the Harrow deck from PF is an excellent tool to "read the cards" for a character and make up an obscure fate for them, that can give the player some direction to explore the sandbox, instead of going around aimlessly.

      -For my recent game, Offline, I created an 8 question procedure for players to brainstorm a kingdom. It usually ends up giving the GM the premise of an adventure, complete with map, good ideas for places to explore and things to happen, as well as a starting point for the players and a good idea of where to go, mostly made up by themselves and a few dice rolls.

      So far, the crushing amount of prep for me has been to create and/or get together all these tools, which even in their actual unrefined state help me a lot to reduce prep time and to surprise my players, even when I use modules.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nHasimir \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Oh... ok
      You asked for details an explanation... so there you have it... it's going to be a big one XD

      - - -

      Well, what I did in Tactical Ops and FateLess was trying to find out the very basic structural elements of the experience I wanted to convey (kind of deconstructing it) and put together tools that would give them to the Players.

      Example...
      The only thing that players are allowed to describe is what the PCs present in the current scene are actively perceiving, right here and now.
      So, no one can say what lays behind a door unless a PC takes action to discover it in the fiction.
      And even then, the ONLY thing that is being described is the PC's perception... it might be flawed, incomplete, misunderstood, a trick or illusion.

      Functionally all Players are in the same exact situation as they would if there was a GM at the table. You (almost) only know what your PC knows, and you only worry about that... the invisible GM will worry about the rest, the story, the big picture... not you... you CAN'T even if you wanted to. You are a Player.

      Then other structures take care of introducing unexpected stuff. But instead of tables of random CONTENT, I use two tables of random SHAPES.
      One (Soft Opposition) only affects the fiction, while the other (Hard Opposition) has also specific mechanical effects.

      As an example here is the Soft Opposition table from the FateLess book...\n

      1) The situation gets sticky
      Your circumstances worsen, someone or something becomes more hostile, or appears now and is already hostile; either way it ACTS against you. It tries.

      2) Your weakness shows
      One of your Aspects causes a BIG PROBLEM for some reason. Doesn\u2019t count as Compel.

      3) Shit hits the fan
      Something important is broken or lost. Someone important (NPC) is apprehended or seriously wounded or dangerously separated from the PCs. Or you deplete an important resource you might need right now or in the near future (ammo, food, drugs, etc).

      4) Something wicked this way comes
      You perceive or are told about something BAD that is happening, or about to happen, here or nearby or somewhere else (but is immediately relevant).

      5) Cut Out
      Something happens to isolate your PC from her allies or an important resource. This does not count as a Compel.

      6) The Ball Drops
      Same as \u201cThe situation gets sticky\u201d but the thing acting against you succeeds in its intent for the current action.
      \nThis way you inject a random SHAPE that the players will have to fill with actual CONTENT.
      It has enough structure so that they Players are not feeling like they are creating a problem for themselves, it just requires that some color be applied to it to make it fit in the current fiction.
      Thanks to this, your random (thus unexpected and fair) element will always look and feel perfectly right, logical and "realistic"... just the same as when a GM springs up with something you did not expect (or expected but did not knew for sure) that is perfectly tied to what has happened so far, and all the current circumstances and details.

      In Tactical Ops such a structure (and other tricks and mechanics) is leveraged to offer a challenge to the PCs.
      They will face Obstacles that have a standard level of difficulty depending on the Mission setup, and then they will have to cope with unexpected events (like in the above table) and with the specific circumstances... because most elements of the fiction can be TAGGED as mechanically relevant, and depending on how you leverage them (or not) they will add good or bad dice to your action rolls.

      Again you have the feeling that an invisible GM has set up a very detailed scenario, that you can interact with, getting logical and realistic and consistent results depending on you ideas and choices and actions.

      - - -

      Of course it is not 100% the same as having an actual GM at the table taking care of all such things with zero input required from the Players.
      But it gets pretty close if you ask me ;-)
      With a few added advantages...

      With a real GM you need a GOOD GM, which means someone with your same aesthetic tastes and priorities AND a decent deal of practice and experience (and time for prep, and the will to put effort into it, each single session) ... it's a valuable commodity that is not always available, right?
      With Tactical Ops and FateLess, you don't need it :P

      Also... in FullMyth you need lots of prepped setting material, and again someone that KNOWS such material and delivers it to the other Players during the game.
      With the right gm-less structure you can generate such material on the fly, but without feeling like you are answering your own questions (boring!) and with all the solidity and consistency and level of detail that your heart might desire.

      It's all a matter of using a series of tricks and mechanics to cheat the Player's perception of the game. You ARE coming up with all the game stuff, but NOT in a "director stance" way, not in a way that makes you feel not-a-Player and too much like a part-time-GM.

      - - -

      What I observed is that most Players often WANT to contribute to the description of situations and places and npcs. It happens all the time. Especially when the system encourages it, or even just makes a bit of space for such contribution.
      And when you DO want to contribute, it doesn't feel like you are the GM, you are just adding a cool idea to the game.
      The "problem" is that if the system asks you to offer this kind of contribution too much, too often, all at once... then yes, you feel like a GM, you start thinking in terms of story and overall picture, not from your PC's perspective.
      This is cool and dandy, but is not what we wanted, right?

      What I did is use tricks to prevent (or at least minimize) this from happening.
      In a 2 Player game you will feel this more, obviously, as whatever I don't say then you have to say. While in a 3+ Player game you feel this exponentially less.

      I hope this rant makes some sort of sense and is somewhat understandable >_<

      \n As an example of a "half myth", "30s ahead" moment, I present "the stolen ring" (from last summer).

      They met an NPC and I decided in that moment that she had a ring that was stolen from a grave and that she would present it as her engagement ring. The guy that she pointed the party to, it was her mothers grave. I was hoping that the party would steal her ring.

      If I had decided that AFTER they had stolen the ring, that would've been bullshit. (But that would've been just the sort of thing I would do in my no myth days!)

      Even deciding as they met her that she had such a ring was a little bit bullshit but at least I decided before the party member stole it from the NPC.

      I don't know.
      It's these sort of interesting secrets and NPC behavior that I love to get from random tables even though I'm pretty good at making them up on the spot.
      Because I don't want to post-hoc too much. It feels like "cheating".

      \n They had backstory with her (she was somebody's Bond) and they met her in a small town where she came to greet them. Maybe I wouldn't have done that now when I have my more randomly driven cities. OTOH it's good to get the Bond into play.

      Her name was Celia.

      Celia says to them "I am engaged, Joane. Won't you take a look at this amazing ring?" bragging.

      This was a lie. She stole the ring from a grave of a woman she did not know, and she suspected they'd steal the ring, and she pointed them towards the woman's sons house and claimed that he was their fiance.

      Joane did steal the ring, which both the DM and the NPC hoped, and went to that house to show it to the guy. (Why would she show it to her rival's fiance though? I don't remember.)
      I do remember the thought "Please steal the ring please steal the ring" going through my head while trying to have poker face to the player so they didn't know that I wanted it.

      He was like "That is my mother's ring you evil!! She was buried with it!". They got in trouble + ten million insp.

      It was 100% linked to their goals which were: "To show up their rival, Celia". Which they later did by disguising themselves as her.

      This was all half myth and I'm more into way fuller myth!

      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2015 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Oh, interesting example. It perfectly showcases the advantages and disadvantages of picking different places on the 'myth' spectrum.

      If you're trying to challenge the players and measure their ability to overcome obstacles, you certainly can't decide that the ring was stolen after they've taken it - that's not playing fair.

      If your goal is to press moral choices on the characters and to interact with their Bonds (BIFTs? I forget), your "prep" (whether it happens, improvised, on the spot, or before the game) very much should interact with their character concepts and choices, because that's your job: to pick up on what the players want to showcase about their characters and to develop story elements which interact with that and create interesting character choices.

      Presenting Celia as the NPC they meet instead of a random villager is a perfect example of that.

      How much of "deciding Celia meets them and has this [story-meaningful] ring" do you think was a problem for you because of technical agenda (you just don't like the feeling of making decisions that way, possibly because you're sick of doing that, for instance) and how much of it was a problem because it made the game less good for everyone (a creative agenda issue)?

      I could see it going either way, depending on the game, and maybe you can't tell right now, but I think it's an interesting question to explore.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n Bond is the B in BIFT a.k.a. TIBF because it's the third one!

      I remember being very happy with the scene at the time. It's only a year later of serendipitous random encounter table choices that I'm casting some TA doubt on it. For my own sake. Everyone at that table (except me) was a Dungeon World player and lover, and it was their first time giving 5e a try. Not that doesn't say so much about the TA they expect for 5e, just because someone loves Dungeon World
      Hey, you mentioned CA, put CA in the ground already!

      I've got to say how very different my enjoyment of our weekly D&D game has been since we put CA in the ground. Really enjoying the three pillars fully (Exploration, Interaction, Combat) with the long RP scenes with the intellectual devourer vs the as-of-yet-uninfected party members, and I'm so digging the author stance that the players switched in and out of with ease.

      \n
      \n Is that the answer? It's a part of it, sure. (And sometimes it's hard to get any more, to be fair.)

      I look for what it feels like to me, how the players react, is it memorable, does it lead to positive social reactions (for example, did people smile and grin and laugh when they saw who they met and what had happened with the ring?), and so forth. Basically, what are people excited about, and to what extent did it come from the choices being made at the table?

      For example, I've played with people where I'm randomizing outcomes and trying to be objective, but they really don't care, because they just want to Be Their Guy, or they want to Hear the GM's Story, or some other thing. But I think that when everyone is genuinely excited about what's going on, it's palpable and easy to spot (if you're paying attention, of course).

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n BIFT and TIBF are joke acronymes for the personal characteristics in D&D 5th edition. Traits, Ideal, Bond and Flaw. Deliverator came up with "BIFT" via Burning Wheels similar name.

      OTOH isn't even technical it just means "on the other hand".
